I wasn’t looking for the crate. I was looking for the pattern. The exception. The truck that arrived without a manifest being waved, the container that was shunted to a specific, quieter corner of the yard.

Hours passed. The sun climbed, baking the gravel. My bad leg stiffened. I shifted my weight, ignored the complaints. The café owner, a man with a tragic face and a magnificent moustache, brought me a second coffee without asking. “Waiting for someone?” he said, wiping his hands on a grimy apron.

“A cousin,” I said. “He drives a truck. Might pass through.”

He nodded, accepting the lie as he accepted most things—with a weary shrug. “Many pass through. Few stop.”

Another hour. Then, I saw it. A unmarked, dark-blue van, not a truck. It entered not through the main gate, but through a smaller, side entrance, one that looked disused, its gate rusted half-shut. The van was clean, out of place among the grimy heavy vehicles. It moved with purpose, directly to a far shed, one separated from the others by a strip of cracked asphalt. A man in grey trousers and a white shirt, not coveralls, emerged from the shed, looked both ways, and waved the van in. The door rolled shut behind it.

Ten minutes later, the van emerged. It left the same way it came. The man in the white shirt stood for a moment, lighting a cigarette, his posture relaxed. A man who had completed a task.

It wasn’t proof. It was a whisper. A deviation in the rhythm. That shed was a quiet room within the noisy machine. I needed a name. A face closer to the action than the man in the white shirt.

I waited until the shift change. The thunder subsided to a rumble as one wave of workers left, another arrived. I saw my chance. A young man, late teens, his coveralls too big, his face smudged with grease. He walked with the exhausted slump of someone at the bottom of the ladder, heading not towards the bus stop with the others, but towards a beat-up moped chained to a fence post. He was alone.

I crossed the road, intercepting him as he fumbled with his keys. He looked up, wary.

“Sorry to bother you,” I said, my French casual, my tone that of a mildly lost bureaucrat. “I’m looking for the dispatcher for the Chénier brothers. The older one, with the bad foot.”

It was a blind guess, a fiction. But every depot has a dispatcher with a bad foot, or a bad temper, or a bad habit.

The boy blinked. “Old Bouchard? He’s not on today. Shift change.”

“Ah,” I said, feigning frustration. I pulled out a few dirham notes, not enough to be a bribe, just enough for a few beers. “Of course. My timing is terrible. Listen, I just need to confirm a shipment number for the customs office. A private van, dark blue. Came in this morning. Would you know which ledger it’s in? It would save me a trip back tomorrow.”

I held the money loosely, my eyes on his. I saw the calculation. The weariness. The desire for the transaction to be over.

He licked his lips, glanced back towards the shed. “Private vans don’t go on the ledger. Not the main one. They go to the little office. Shed Seven. Marcel handles those.”

“Marcel?”
“The guy in the white shirt. He’s the brother’s nephew or something.” The boy snatched the money, quick as a bird. “That’s all I know. Don’t say I told you.”

He unlocked his moped, kicked it to life, and puttered away in a cloud of blue smoke.

Marcel. Shed Seven. A nephew. It was a start. A thread, thin as a hair.

I didn’t approach Shed Seven. That would be suicide. Instead, I retreated, circling the depot’s perimeter at a distance, noting the roads in and out, the blind spots in the chain-link. I found a vantage point on a low rise of scrubland, where I could watch the shed through a small pair of binoculars I’d bought from a market stall days before. I watched Marcel leave at dusk, locking the shed with a heavy padlock. He got into a small, cream-coloured Citroën and drove towards the city, not towards the workers’ districts.

I followed on foot, as far as I could, losing him in the thickening traffic but marking the direction: towards the newer, wealthier suburbs in the foothills. Not a foreman’s neighbourhood. A nephew’s neighbourhood.

The walk back to the Habous was long, the pain in my leg a burning brand with every step. The city had turned on its evening lights, a false, cheerful glitter over the deepening gloom. I bought a bottle of water and a loaf of bread from a corner stall, ate without tasting.

Back in the room under the eaves, I spread a blank page from my notebook on the small table. In the centre, I drew a box. The Villa. From it, I drew a line. The Crate. From the crate, a line back to Shed 7. From Shed 7, I wrote Marcel (nephew). From Marcel, an arrow pointing to The Brothers Chénier?.

It was a pathetic sketch. A child’s drawing of a spiderweb. But it was a beginning. The clean man’s world was one of insulation. Layers of protocol, of cut-outs, of deniability. Marcel was a weakness. Family was always a weakness. It introduced loyalty, fear, sentiment—variables that couldn’t be fully controlled. A nephew might be privileged, but he was also a liability. He knew things. He might have ambitions, or resentments, that stretched beyond his assigned role.

I needed to get to Marcel, but not at the depot. Not on his uncle’s territory. I needed him in the city, in the space between his two lives. I needed leverage.

The next morning, I began the patient, tedious work of building a ghost. I used the post office box. I made a call from a public phone near the Marché Central, to a contact in Tangier I hadn’t spoken to in a year—a forger of quiet genius who owed me a favour from a mess involving a stolen passport and a Belgian diplomat’s wife. I requested a document, a specific kind of credential. He asked no questions, only gave me a time and a place for the drop.

Then, I went to the library. The public library was a grand, dusty hall, a relic of the Protectorate’s civilizing mission. I requested back issues of local business registries, trade directories. I looked for the name Chénier. I found it. Frères Chénier – Transport et Logistique. Founded 1948. Directors: Philippe Chénier and Gérard Chénier. An address in the commercial district. A photograph from a society page, two middle-aged men in suits, cutting a ribbon at the opening of a new warehouse, 1954. They were smiling the tight smiles of men who had prospered by knowing which way the wind was blowing before it changed.

I looked for Marcel. He wasn’t in the directories. But in a later society photo, a charity gala for the Moroccan Red Crescent in 1957, I found him. Standing slightly behind and to the left of Uncle Philippe. A young man with a slick of dark hair and a smile that didn’t reach his eyes, holding a glass of champagne as if it were a tool he hadn’t yet learned to use. The caption read: “...with M. Marcel Thibault.”

Thibault. Not Chénier. A nephew on the mother’s side. An outsider, even in the family business.

I closed the heavy tome, the dust motes dancing in a shaft of pale library light. Marcel Thibault. A man with two names, standing in two worlds. A perfect point of pressure.

The drop from Tangier came that evening, left in a locker at the Gare des Voyageurs. A slim envelope. Inside was a perfect facsimile of an identity card for the Direction de la Surveillance du Territoire—the DST, Morocco’s nascent internal security service. The photo was mine. The name was not. It was a dangerous toy. A lit match in a room full of gas. But sometimes, you need a brighter light to see the true shape of the shadows.

I didn’t want to arrest Marcel. I wanted to scare him. To make him believe the insulation was failing. That the clean man’s protection had a crack, and through it, a colder, more bureaucratic wind was blowing.

I tracked his routine for two days. He was a creature of habit. He left the depot each day at six. He drove his cream Citroën to a café in the Habous, of all places—the Café des Arcades, just ten minutes’ walk from my pension. He would sit at an outside table, drink an espresso, read the newspaper. He was always alone. He never looked like he enjoyed it. It was a ritual, a performance of a certain kind of life.

On the third day, I made my move.

I wore a plain, dark suit I’d bought from a second-hand stall. I carried a thin leather briefcase. I looked like what I was pretending to be: a minor functionary with the power to ruin a man’s day. I arrived at the Café des Arcades just as Marcel was folding his newspaper. I walked directly to his table and sat down without invitation.

He looked up, startled, then annoyed. “This table is taken.”

“I know, Monsieur Thibault,” I said, my voice low, flat. I placed the DST identity card on the table between us, just for a moment, then slid it back into my inner pocket. I didn’t let him study it, only see enough to register the seal, the official look. Fear works best with a glimpse, not an examination.

His face drained of colour. The annoyance vanished, replaced by a primal, gut-level alarm. His eyes flicked left and right.

“Don’t,” I said. “This is a quiet conversation. Or it can be a loud one. It depends on you.”

“What do you want?” His voice was a dry whisper.

“Information. Not about your uncles. About one specific client. The one who uses the blue van. The one who gets the special handling in Shed Seven.”

He swallowed. “I don’t know what you’re talking about. We have many clients.”

“You have one client who pays in something other than cash. In protection. In silence. I want a name. Not the company name. The man’s name.”

“I can’t.” It was a reflex, weak.

“You can,” I said, leaning forward slightly. “Because the alternative is that I walk away from this table and the next people who visit you won’t sit down. They will pull you from your bed at three in the morning. They will take you to a room with no windows. And they will ask you about everything. Not just the blue van. About the gasoline siphoned from your uncles’ trucks and sold on the black market. About the customs forms you’ve been falsifying for the past eighteen months. About the girl from the cigarette factory your wife knows nothing about.” I was fishing, throwing out hooks based on the universal frailties of men in his position. But the hooks bit. I saw each one land in his eyes—a flinch of guilt, a tremor of fear.

He was breathing quickly now, a trapped animal. “They will kill me.”

“They might,” I agreed, my tone conversational. “But the state will definitely imprison you. Your uncles will disown you. Your wife will leave you. It will be a very public, very unpleasant end. Or, you can give me a name. A name I will never trace back to you. And this goes no further. The DST has bigger fish to fry than a frightened nephew with sticky fingers.”

The lie was smooth, oily. I watched him wrestle with it. The café sounds around us—the clink of cups, the murmur of conversation—seemed to recede, leaving us in a bubble of terrible silence.

“He’s not a client,” Marcel whispered finally, his eyes on the table. “He’s a partner. A silent partner.”

“His name.”
“I’ve only heard a name. Once. My uncle Philippe was on the telephone. He was angry. He said… he said, ‘Rousseau does not own the roads.’”
Rousseau. A common name. A French name. It meant nothing. And everything.

“Describe him.”
“I’ve never met him! I swear. The van drivers, they are different each time. They say nothing. The instructions come on typed paper. No signature.”

“Where does the van go after your shed?”
“I don’t know. To the port, I assume. But not to the main gates. There is a private gate, near the old customs house. Gate C. It is never used. Except for them.”

Gate C. Another coordinate.

I leaned back. “You will go to work tomorrow. You will do nothing different. If you warn your uncle, if you change one single thing about your routine, the next visit will not be so polite. Do you understand?”

He nodded, a quick, jerky motion.

I stood up, dropped a few coins on the table for his untouched coffee. “Enjoy the rest of your evening, Monsieur Thibault.”

I walked away, not looking back, feeling his terrified gaze on my spine until I turned the corner. My heart was a hammer against my ribs. The performance had been a high-wire act. One slip, one moment of doubt in his eyes, and it would have collapsed.

Back in my room, I added to the map in my notebook. Rousseau (silent partner). Gate C (port, old customs house). The lines were connecting. The shape was still vague, but it was no longer a ghost. It had a French name and a private gate.

Rousseau. A clean name. A name that could belong to a bureaucrat, a businessman, a retired colonel. A name that could own a villa with a dry fountain.

I had a name. But a name without a face, without a history, was just a noise. I needed to give it substance. I needed to find the crack in Rousseau’s world, the equivalent of a frightened nephew. Everyone has a weakness. Even clean men.

The night was deep and still. I sat by the window, watching the sickly orange tree in the courtyard below. It was then I remembered Léa’s words, from what felt like a lifetime ago, in the damp cellar of the villa. She had been talking about the people who moved the crate, the people who asked no questions. She’d said: “They are not monsters. They are men with keys. The monster is the lock.”

Rousseau was a man with keys. Many keys. To villas, to private gates, to silent partnerships. But a lock implies something locked away. A secret. A truth. My job was no longer to find the crate. It was to find the lock that Rousseau’s key fitted. And then to pick it.

The first step was Gate C. The private port entrance. The clean, quiet artery into the heart of the official machinery. That was where Rousseau’s world touched the city’s. That was where I would find the seam.

I had a name. I had a gate. I had twenty-four hours left, not to disappear, but to penetrate. The battlefield was no longer an outline. It was a map with a single, glaring entry point. And I was going to walk right through it.

The hook for the next chapter was the gate itself. It was both an opportunity and a potential trap, a known point of vulnerability that Rousseau might have deliberately left visible. Walking towards it felt like stepping into a sniper’s sightline, but it was the only line I had.



---

Chapter 16

CHAPTER 16

Gate C was a fiction. It was a bureaucratic euphemism stamped on a customs form, a neat line on a port authority schematic. In the flesh, under the flat, metallic dawn light bleeding over the Anfa district, it was a contradiction: a gap masquerading as a barrier.

It sat at the end of a spur of cracked asphalt that curled away from the main port road like an afterthought. On one side, the high, stained concrete wall of the old German consulate warehouse, pockmarked with old bullet scars from a forgotten skirmish. On the other, a rusted chain-link fence, sagging under the weight of decades of salt-wind and neglect, offering a gauzy view of stacked shipping containers and the skeletal gantries beyond. Between them stood two concrete pillars, painted a faded institutional green. No gate hung between them. Just empty air, and a single, weather-beaten sign bolted to the right-hand pillar: ACCÈS INTERDIT – PERSONNEL AUTORISÉ SEULEMENT. The prohibition was an antique. The authority it invoked had evaporated with the Protectorate.

This was the seam. Not a guarded checkpoint, but a forgotten hyphen between jurisdictions. The port’s security was a nervous, new thing, concentrated on the main gates where trucks lined up for inspection. This was a back door left ajar by history. Rousseau hadn’t built it. He had simply found it, recognized its useful obscurity, and hung his own, invisible lock on it.

I stood a hundred yards back, in the lee of a derelict customs shed that smelled of pigeon droppings and old motor oil. My leg approved of the stillness. The pain was a low, constant hum, the background static of my existence. I lit a cigarette, not for the nicotine, but for the cover of the gesture, the excuse to stand and watch. The smoke was bitter in the damp air.

For an hour, nothing moved through the gap but the wind, carrying the port’s symphony of clanging metal, distant shouts, and the deep-throated groan of a ship’s horn. Then, at seven-thirty, the tempo changed. A flatbed Citroën Traction Avant, its black paint dull with dust, emerged from the container maze and glided through the pillars without a pause. It turned north, away from the port, and disappeared into the morning traffic on the boulevard. Ten minutes later, a man on a bicycle, a ledger book strapped to the rear rack, pedaled sedately through. He wore the blue coveralls of the port authority, but his route was wrong. He should have been heading into the heart of the docks, not leaving via a disused spur.

They were not monsters. They were men with keys. The key was knowledge. The knowledge that Gate C was not a gate, but a habit. A worn path through the bureaucratic undergrowth.

I needed to walk that path. But not as myself. A lone European limping through that empty archway would be a flare in the fog. I needed to be part of the furniture.

I spent the morning in the Marché Central, among the ice-packed stalls of gleaming fish and the cries of the vendors. I bought a pair of stained, loose-fitting trousers, a thick blue cotton work shirt, and a cheap, billed cap the colour of dried mud. From a hardware stall, I acquired a heavy metal toolbox, empty, its latch rusted shut. The weight was good. It gave my limp a purpose, a reason. A man with a tool, going to fix something. The oldest disguise in the world.

By midday, I was back in the shadow of the shed. The rhythm had established itself. Movement through the gap was sporadic, never hurried, always purposeful. It was a back channel. I waited for a pulse. A small Renault van went through, followed by two men walking together, deep in conversation. I fell in behind them at a distance, letting my toolbox swing heavily with each step, my head down, my gait a slow, grinding testament to a bad back, a bad leg, a life of hard use.

The concrete pillars grew larger. Up close, the green paint was flaking like diseased skin. I could see the old bolt holes where a gate once swung. I crossed the threshold.

The world did not change. The asphalt continued, littered with wind-blown trash and weeds pushing through the cracks. To the left, the high warehouse wall. To the right, through the chain-link, the port proper: the ordered chaos of cranes and containers. But the atmosphere had a different density. The sounds were muffled, filtered. I was in the interstitial zone, the space the maps ignored.

A hundred yards in, the spur ended at a T-junction. To the right, a locked steel door set into the warehouse wall. To the left, the road narrowed into a lane that ran behind a row of low, single-story buildings with corrugated iron roofs. These were the old customs houses, built by the French for a more meticulous, paper-driven form of control. Most were shuttered, windows boarded. One, at the far end, showed signs of life: a clean, recently-installed metal door, painted dark grey, and a single window, its glass washed, flanked by two healthy geraniums in red clay pots.

A villa with a dry fountain. A customs house with geraniums.

I turned left, my boots crunching on the gravel shoulder. The toolbox was a lead weight in my hand. I passed the first building. The second. I kept my eyes on the ground ahead, the picture of a labourer on a dispiriting errand. As I neared the maintained building, I let the toolbox slip from my grip. It hit the ground with a solid, metallic clang that seemed to tear the quiet air.

I cursed, low and guttural, and bent to retrieve it, my movements slow and pained. From my crouch, I had a clear view beneath the window sill. The ground was swept clean. No dust, no debris. And set into the foundation, just visible, was a modern electrical conduit, white PVC, snaking its way into the earth. It was too substantial for a light bulb. It was the kind of conduit you ran for dedicated power. For a freezer unit. Or a climate-controlled space.

The door opened.

A man stood in the frame, not filling it, but occupying it with a quiet, complete authority. He was in his late fifties, lean, dressed in immaculate fawn-coloured trousers and a crisp white shirt, sleeves rolled precisely to the elbows. His hair was steel-grey, cut close. His face was narrow, intelligent, devoid of curiosity. He held a porcelain coffee cup in one hand.

“You are lost,” he said. His French was Parisian, clean and cold as a surgical blade. It wasn’t a question.

I straightened up, wincing for effect, and touched the brim of my cap. “The foreman sent me. For the refrigeration line in shed seven. He said the junction box was back here.”

The man took a slow sip of his coffee. His eyes, a pale, watery blue, did not leave my face. They took inventory: the cheap clothes, the toolbox, the leg, the face. They were the eyes of a man who had spent a lifetime assessing value and risk on balance sheets and in shadowed rooms.

“There is no shed seven on this lane,” he said. “You have mistaken the gate.”

“Gate C, he said.”

“This is not Gate C. Gate C is a administrative designation. This is private property.” He said it without emphasis, a simple recitation of fact. “You will turn around. You will follow the lane back to the pillars. You will not return.”

There was no threat in his tone. There didn’t need to be. The threat was in the geraniums, in the clean conduit, in the absolute, unassailable certainty of his presence. This was Rousseau. It couldn’t be anyone else. He was the lock, standing in his own doorway.

I nodded, a dumb, obedient gesture. “My mistake, monsieur. Sorry to disturb.”

I turned, my limp more pronounced, and began the long walk back down the lane, feeling the weight of his gaze between my shoulder blades. I didn’t look back. I counted my steps. At forty-seven, I heard the soft click of his door closing.

He had shown himself. A calculated risk. To him, I was a piece of port trash, a mistaken labourer. Showing himself was more efficient than hiding. It established dominion. It ended curiosity. For a normal man, it would have. It would have sent him shuffling back to the foreman, confused and chastened.

But he had given me the face. And in that face, I had seen the history. It was not the face of a brute or a thug. It was the face of an administrator. A comptroller. The kind of man who had managed the flow of goods—and perhaps, during the war, the flow of other things—from behind a desk in Vichy or Algiers or right here in Casablanca. The Protectorate had ended, but the mechanisms remained. The men who knew the levers had simply found new weights to lift.

I passed back through the pillars, back into the noisy, common world. My heart was a slow, heavy drum. I had found him. More importantly, I had seen his weakness. His weakness was his cleanliness. His order. The geraniums were a tell. He needed control, a perimeter free of dust and doubt. My appearance was a speck of dust. He had removed it, politely, firmly. But a man who fears dust spends his life watching for it. It makes him predictable. It makes him focus on the perimeter, not the core.

I needed to get inside that clean, grey door. Not as a labourer. As something else. As a piece of paperwork, as a bureaucratic anomaly. As a problem that couldn’t be dismissed with a word.

Back in my rented room, the map in my notebook grew new lines. I drew the lane, the customs houses, the grey door. I wrote: Rousseau – comptroller. Fastidious. Perimeter-obsessed. Weakness: dust.

Léa’s words came back, not as a comfort, but as a confirmation. The monster is the lock. Rousseau was both. He was the man with the key, and the lock he protected. To pick it, I needed to understand the mechanism. I needed to know what flowed through that back channel, through Gate C, that required a man like Rousseau to stand sentry over geraniums.

The crate from the villa was gone, moved. It would have come here. This was the next node. But a node for what? Storage? Transfer? Modification?

I thought of the electrical conduit. Climate control. Not for art. Art could tolerate a range of humours. But some things required a specific, stable environment. Precision instruments. Delicate chemicals. Biological specimens.

The silence around Rousseau was not just professional discretion. It was the silence of a specialized, technical process. He wasn’t a warehouse thug. He was a foreman of a silent, delicate assembly line.

I had twenty-two hours left. The battlefield had contracted from a city to a lane, from a network to a single, grey door. Penetrating it head-on was suicide. I had to become part of its system. I had to be a document that landed on his desk, a discrepancy in his ledger that required his personal attention.

The answer came from the past. From the very bureaucracy he had mastered. I needed a counterpart. A phantom from the new administration, clumsy and officious, asking the wrong questions in the right way. I needed to create a draft, and let it blow under his door.

I spent the evening forging a letterhead. It was not difficult. The new Moroccan port authority was a patchwork of old French forms and new Arabic script, rife with minor departments and overlapping committees. I created the Bureau de Vérification des Installations Anciennes. The Office for the Verification of Antiquated Installations. It was the kind of pointless, pedantic committee that bloomed in the aftermath of empire, staffed by junior officials making work for themselves. The letter was a masterpiece of benign obstruction. It cited a new (non-existent) regulation regarding the electrical safety of pre-1940 port structures. It requested, in politely insistent terms, a mandatory inspection of the electrical grounding in all structures on the former Gate C spur, to be scheduled within 48 hours. It was signed by a M. Hassan El-Fassi, a name so common it was virtually invisible.

The letter was my pick. It was dust, of a very specific kind. The kind that came with a triplicate form.

At dawn, I was back at the Marché Central, in different clothes. I paid a young boy, all sharp eyes and hungry grin, fifty francs. I gave him the envelope, addressed to Le Responsable, Entrepôt Ancien, Voie d’Accès C. I told him to slide it under the grey door with the geraniums, and to run. He did it for the theatre of it, disappearing down the lane like a shadow.

Now, I waited. The trap was not for Rousseau. The trap was for his routine. A man of order must respond to disorder. He would have to verify the letter, make a call, perhaps even visit the port authority to quietly squash this nuisance. He would have to move. And when he moved, his clean, grey room would be unattended. Not for long. Ten minutes. Perhaps fifteen.

It was a gamble on his fastidiousness. A man who loved control would not leave an official inquiry, however spurious, festering. He would resolve it. Efficiently. Personally.

I took up a new position, in the upper floor of a half-demolished building overlooking the main boulevard north of the port, the route he would likely take to the administration block. I had a pair of opera glasses I’d bought from a pawn shop in the medina. They were scratched, but they brought the world into sharp, trembling focus.

The morning bled into afternoon. The pain in my leg settled into a deep, bony ache. I watched the traffic flow, a sluggish river of vehicles. Then, at three-seventeen, the black Citroën Traction Avant from that morning emerged from the side street that led to the Gate C spur. It turned onto the boulevard, heading east, toward the city centre and the main port offices.

Rousseau was driving. Alone. His profile was sharp, focused ahead. He was going to kill the letter.

I gave the car two minutes to disappear into the traffic. Then I moved.

My descent from the ruin was a clumsy agony of rubble and rusted rebar. I hit the street and began to walk, not run, toward the spur. The toolbox was gone. In its place, I carried a leather satchel, the kind a low-level engineer or surveyor might use. I wore the same cap, pulled low.

The pillars passed. The lane was deserted. The geraniums were a violent splash of red in the grey afternoon. The grey door looked impregnable.

I didn’t try the door. I went to the window. The lock was a simple pin-tumbler, old but well-maintained. My picks were cold in my fingers. The first pin set. The second. My hearing stretched, filtering the distant port noise for any footfall on gravel. The third pin. The fourth. The plug turned with a soft, oiled click.

I slid the window up, just enough to pass through. The room inside was dim, cool, and smelled of coffee, lemon polish, and something else—a faint, antiseptic odour, like a hospital corridor.

I closed the window behind me and stood still, letting my eyes adjust.

It was an office, but unlike any I had seen. It was monastic in its order. A large, empty desk of pale oak. A single telephone. A blotter, perfectly centred. A wire inbox, empty. A wire outbox, empty. Two filing cabinets against the far wall, their drawers flush. No pictures. No personal items of any kind. It was a stage set for a play about efficiency.

The antiseptic smell was stronger here. It came from a second door, set into the back wall. This one was heavier, modern, with a rubber seal around its frame. It had a keycard lock, a small green LED glowing beside it.

Rousseau’s inner sanctum. The lock within the lock.

I went to the desk first. The drawers were unlocked. They contained nothing but stationery: pens, pencils, a ruler, all aligned. A bottle of black ink. It was a parody of an office. Then, in the bottom right drawer, I found it. A single, slim ledger, bound in black leather. I opened it.

It was not written in French or Arabic. It was a series of codes, alphanumeric strings, and times. Next to each entry was a weight, in kilograms, and a temperature range: +2°C to +8°C. Then a destination: three-letter codes I recognized as airport identifiers. TLS. MRS. GVA. And a final column: a six-digit number, always beginning with ‘40’ or ‘41’.

Bank account numbers. Swiss.

This was the ledger of the lock. It recorded what passed through Gate C. Not crates. Not art. Consignments. Temperature-controlled consignments, shipped to European airports. Paid for via Zurich or Geneva.

I heard the low growl of an engine in the lane.

My blood turned to ice. It was too soon. He couldn’t be back.

I snapped the ledger shut, replaced it exactly, closed the drawer. The engine idled, then cut. A car door opened and closed.

Not the Citroën. A different engine sound. Rougher.

I was trapped. The window was my only exit, and it opened onto the lane, right where the new arrival would be. I looked at the sealed door. The keycard lock was impossible. I was in a clean, grey box.

Footsteps on the gravel. One person. Heavy, unhurried.

My eyes swept the sterile room. There was nowhere to hide. Under the desk? He would see me the moment he entered.

The footsteps stopped outside the main door. A key rattled in the lock.

There was one place. The one place a fastidious man would never look, because it was an extension of himself. I moved behind the desk, not crouching, but standing straight, in the shadowed corner between the desk and the wall. It was the servant’s position. The place a subordinate would stand, waiting for instruction. It was the only piece of the room not governed by pure geometry.

The door opened. Light from the lane spilled in, framing a large, broad-shouldered silhouette. It was not Rousseau. This man filled the doorway, his head nearly brushing the top. He stepped inside, letting the door swing shut behind him. He wore a leather jacket over a workman’s shirt. His face was in shadow, but I could see the blunt, powerful line of his jaw. He didn’t turn on the light. He knew the room.

He walked directly to the sealed door. From his pocket, he produced a white keycard. He swiped it. The LED blinked from green to red, and there was a solid thunk as the magnetic lock disengaged. He pulled the heavy door open.

A wave of cold, antiseptic air washed out, carrying with it a new smell: the sharp, coppery scent of blood, and beneath it, something sweet and cloying, like formaldehyde.

The man disappeared inside. The door began to swing slowly shut on its pneumatic hinge. In the sliver of light from within, I saw a glimpse of stainless steel, the gleam of glass containers, the soft hum of machinery.

Then the door sealed with a definitive sigh, and the LED returned to green.

The lock was not for art. It was for a cold room. A laboratory. Or a butcher’s shop. Or both.

I had ten seconds. Maybe less. I crossed the room in three silent strides, opened the main door, and stepped out into the lane, pulling the door closed just as the latch clicked home behind me. I didn’t look back. I walked, my satchel over my shoulder, my pace measured, down the lane, past the dead buildings, toward the pillars. The afternoon sun felt thin and insubstantial on my skin.

I had picked the first lock. I had seen the monster. It was not a crate, or a man. It was a process, cold and clinical, running on Swiss time and recorded in a sterile ledger. Rousseau was its conductor. The man in the leather jacket was its hand.

And I had just become a piece of dust in its immaculate machinery. They would find the opened window lock. They would know. The phantom letter from a phantom bureau would now have a very real, very dangerous consequence.

I had a name. I had a face. I had a ledger of codes and temperatures and bank accounts. And I had, irrevocably, stepped out of the shadows and into the sterile, frozen light of their attention. The hunt was no longer one-sided. I had rattled the cage. Now the thing inside was awake, and it knew my smell.

The hook for the next chapter was the ledger. It was a key, but a key that burned to hold. The codes were a map to a network far wider than Casablanca, a network that dealt in cold, biological currency. Using it would mean stepping fully into the light, declaring war on a system that operated in the deep freeze of total silence. I was no longer a ghost haunting a villa. I was a contaminant in a clean room. And they had protocols for contamination.



---

Chapter 17

CHAPTER 17

The ledger was a block of ice in my hands. I sat in the back of a taxi, a battered Mercedes that smelled of clove cigarettes and damp wool, watching the city blur past the rain-streaked window. The driver had asked no questions when I’d given the address, a small hotel in the Ain Diab district, far from the villa and farther from the port. The tempo here was different. The wide corniche road was for strolling, for seeing and being seen, but the rain had emptied it, leaving only the skeletal outlines of beach clubs and the grey, heaving mass of the Atlantic beyond the seawall. The pain in my leg was a constant, low-grade hum, synced to the throb of the wipers.

I paid the driver in cash, took the stairs to the third floor one agonizing step at a time, and let myself into the room. It was a spare, clean cell: a narrow bed, a desk of pale wood, a window overlooking a narrow interior courtyard where a single, soaked palm tree rattled in the wind. I placed the ledger on the desk. It was a standard commercial journal, bound in black pebbled leather, the pages ruled in neat blue lines. From the outside, it promised nothing more thrilling than inventory counts or freight manifests.

I opened it.

The handwriting was a model of bureaucratic precision. Rousseau’s, I assumed. Each entry was a line of cipher. Not a language, but a code. Alphanumeric strings, separated by dashes and decimal points. GR-7.2a, LK-4.1, SV-9.0. Next to each, a temperature range: -18°C to -22°C, -70°C, +4°C. Then, a time in UTC, a flight number or a ship’s name, and a bank routing number with a corresponding sum in Swiss francs. The sums were not outrageous. They were discreet, professional fees. This was not a ledger of grand theft. It was a ledger of logistics. Of procurement and delivery. The monster was not a single beast; it was a supply chain.

I traced a finger down the page. The codes were the key. The temperatures told a story. -70°C was deep freeze, the kind used for tissue samples, for viruses, for living cells that needed to be suspended, not dead. -18°C was for transport, for stable, non-living biological material. +4°C was refrigeration, for something that needed to remain active, like blood or certain pharmaceuticals.

I leaned back, the chair creaking. The colonial aftermath was not just maps redrawn and ministries renamed. It was this: the infrastructure left behind, the cold rooms in the villas of the departed, the Swiss bank accounts kept open, the quiet understanding that some systems were too useful, too profitable, to be dismantled by something as messy as independence. Rousseau was a ghost of the protectorate, conducting his symphony of chill in the basement of a new nation.

My satchel lay on the bed. Inside, wrapped in oilcloth, was the Luger. It was weight. It was an answer to a kind of question. But the questions this ledger posed were not of that sort. You could not shoot a temperature. You could not ambush a bank transfer.

I had to break the code.

I started with the obvious. GR could be Geneva. LK could be Lukavac, in Bosnia, or perhaps a person’s initials. SV could be Svizzera—Switzerland—or something else entirely. It was a rabbit hole of infinite depth. I needed a crib, a single point of reference to anchor this constellation of cold facts.

The last entry in the ledger was dated three days prior. SV-9.0. Temperature: +4°C. Transport: Air Maroc 804. Account: Banque Privée, Zürich. The destination was blank.

Air Maroc 804 was a daily flight. Casablanca to Paris, Orly.

I looked at my watch. It was nearly six. Flight 804 would have landed hours ago. Whatever SV-9.0 was, it was now in Europe, being unpacked in some anonymous warehouse near Orly, its temperature carefully maintained.

I needed to get to Paris.

The thought was a fresh wave of fatigue. Paris was a city of other ghosts, of other aftermaths. My French was passable, my leg was a liability, and my face was now known to a man in a leather jacket who worked for a man who kept a sterile ledger. Traveling under my own passport was an invitation. But the ledger was useless here. It was a snapshot of a machine in motion. To understand it, I had to see the next cog turn.

There was a knock at the door.

It was not the sharp rap of hotel staff. It was a single, firm impact, followed by silence.

I moved without sound, the Luger finding my hand from the oilcloth as naturally as a breath. I stood to the side of the door, my back to the wall. The pain in my leg sharpened, a spike of protest.

“Monsieur?” A woman’s voice, low, edged with a tension that vibrated through the wood. “I am a friend of Kader’s.”

Kader. The fixer from the Place des Nations Unies, a lifetime ago in Chapter 1. The man who existed in the porous space. I had not seen him since that first week, when he had provided the initial, useless whispers about the villa. His friendship was a currency of uncertain value.

I said nothing.

“He said you might need a door,” she said. “A new door. One that does not look behind.”

A passport. A way out.

I slid the bolt back, slowly, and opened the door a few inches, the Luger held low and out of sight.

She was in her forties, perhaps. Her hair was covered by a navy tarbouche, a fine wool overcoat draped over her shoulders. Her face was striking, not for beauty, but for its composed severity, the kind carved by hard decisions made in quiet rooms. Her eyes took me in, the room behind me, the space at my side where she knew a weapon would be. She did not flinch.

“You are the man with the bad leg,” she said. It was not a question.

“And you are?”

“Samira. Kader is… unavailable. He asked me to look in. He heard a certain villa had a window open today.” She glanced past me, at the ledger open on the desk. “You found the bookkeeper’s bible.”

“Come in,” I said, stepping back.

She entered, her movements economical. She did not sit. She went to the window, looked down at the sodden courtyard, then turned. “They will be looking for a limping European. They will have descriptions. They will have friends at the ports, at the airport. The train to Tangier will be watched. The new Morocco has many eyes, and some of them still work for the old Morocco.”

“I need to get to Paris.”

A faint, grim smile touched her lips. “Of course you do. The last delivery. You think it will explain the rest.”

“It’s a place to start.”

“It is a place to die,” she said flatly. “Rousseau does not work alone in Paris. His network is like mycelium. It is under everything. You go to Orly, you ask questions about a refrigerated cargo, and you will be a stain on a tarmac before sunset.”

“Then what do you suggest?”

She studied me for a long moment. “You have the ledger. That makes you a threat. But a threat is also a lever. You do not follow the shipment. You intercept the sender.”

“Rousseau.”

“He will be… agitated. His system is pristine. You are a flaw. He will want to contain you. To sterilize the error.” She took a step closer. Her scent was of orange blossom and cold air. “He will not run. He is a man of routine, of control. His villa is his fortress. But a fortress can be a prison. You have seen his cold room. You have his book. He does not know what else you have. Use that.”

“Walk back into the lion’s den.”

“You already picked his lock. He will not expect you to knock on the front gate.” She reached into her coat and produced a folded slip of paper. “A door. As promised. A Belgian cargo pilot. He flies antiquities out of Marrakesh, sometimes other things. He leaves from a private strip near Settat at dawn. He is going to Le Bourget, not Orly. He has been… incentivized to have a mechanical delay that requires an extra passenger seat. You will be a mechanic, hitching a ride for a repair part.”

I took the paper. Coordinates, a time, a name: Filip.

“And in return?” I asked.

“In return, you take this with you.” From another pocket, she produced a small, sealed aluminum tube, the kind used for photographic film. “You give it to a man at the Café de la Paix, near the Opera, at noon the day after you land. You will be approached. You will say ‘Kader sent the spring water.’ He will take the tube.”

“What is it?”

“That is not your concern. It is a counter-weight. A piece of dust for their machinery.” Her eyes held mine. “We are all ghosts here, monsieur. Haunting the spaces between what was and what is promised. Sometimes, ghosts must work together.”

She turned to leave.

“Samira.”

She paused at the door.

“Why? Kader owed me nothing.”

“Kader owes everyone everything. It is how he survives.” She looked back, and the severity in her face softened, just for an instant, into something like pity. “And because the thing in Rousseau’s basement? It is not for export. Not all of it. Some of it is for here. For us. New diseases for the new country. We have enough of our own.”

Then she was gone, the door clicking shut behind her, leaving only the scent of orange blossom and the silence of the rain.

I looked at the tube. It was light. It was everything I didn’t know. I packed the ledger, the Luger, the tube. I left the hotel key on the desk. The taxi to Settat would take two hours, if I could find one willing to go in the rain, in the dark.

The private strip was a scar of tarmac laid across a flat, rocky plain. The dawn was a grey smear in the east, the rain reduced to a persistent, misting drizzle. A single hanger stood off to the side, its doors open, yellow light spilling out to illuminate a twin-prop Cessna looking like a tired, metal insect. A man in a leather flight jacket stood under a wing, smoking, his face illuminated in the glow of his cigarette.

I approached, my bag heavy on my shoulder, my leg screaming from the walk from the main road.

“Filip?” I called out.

The man flicked his cigarette into the wet darkness. “You’re the mechanic.” His French was accented, his tone bored. “Get in. We’re behind schedule as it is.”

The flight was a study in vibration and cold. I sat in the co-pilot’s seat, a headset over my ears, the drone of the engines a physical pressure. Filip did not speak. He chewed gum and watched the instruments, a man performing a mundane chore. Below, the grey-green folds of the Atlas Mountains gave way to the hazy expanse of the Mediterranean, then the geometric patterns of southern France. The ledger was in my bag, at my feet. The tube was in my pocket.

Le Bourget was a chaos of small private craft. Filip taxied to a distant, anonymous hanger, cut the engines, and turned to me. “End of the line, mechanic. The part is in the back. Don’t linger.”

I climbed out, the cold air of France a shock after the damp chill of Morocco. It was sharper, drier. I walked across the tarmac, a solitary figure with a limp, carrying a satchel. No one looked at me twice.

Paris was a different kind of maze. I took a bus, then the Metro, surfacing near Montmartre. I found a cheap hotel off the Rue Lepic, a room with faded floral wallpaper and a window that looked onto a light well. The sounds were different here: the higher pitch of French voices, the specific rumble of Parisian traffic, the absence of the call to prayer.

I had until noon the next day.

I opened the ledger again. In the flat, electric light of the Parisian room, the codes seemed even more sterile. SV-9.0. In Paris. Somewhere.

I had a name. Rousseau. I had a face. The man in the leather jacket. And I had a ledger that connected them to a bank in Zürich and a flight to Orly.

But Samira was right. Walking into Orly was suicide. Rousseau was the source. The villa was the heart. I had to make the heart react.

I spent the evening crafting a message. It was a single line, typed on a sheet of hotel stationery on a machine I found in a tabac. A line of cipher from the ledger, one of the older entries: GR-7.2a. And below it, a temperature: -70°C. And a question mark.

I placed it in a plain envelope. The address was the villa in Casablanca. I paid a street kid to mail it, from a post office across the city.

It was a stone dropped into a still, deep pond. The ripples would take time to reach me. But they would come.

The next morning, the rain had caught up with me. A soft, Parisian drizzle glossed the cobblestones of the Rue de la Paix. The Café de la Paix was a grand, old-world affair, all brass and mirrors and white linen. I took a table outside under the awning, my back to the wall, a view of the square and the Opera beyond. I ordered a coffee. My leg was a solid ache, a barometer of the impending storm.

At noon precisely, a man sat down at the table next to mine. He was elderly, dressed in an immaculate, if slightly old-fashioned, grey suit. He ordered a citron pressé. He unfolded a newspaper.

I waited. My coffee grew cold.

After five minutes, he spoke without looking up from his paper. “The water from the spring. Is it cool?”

“Kader sent the spring water,” I said, my voice low.

He gave a slight nod. His hand, liver-spotted and steady, reached over. I placed the aluminum tube on the tablecloth. His fingers closed over it, and it vanished into his coat.

“You have been to the source,” he said, still pretending to read.

“Yes.”

“It is deeper than you know. The cold room is just the antechamber.” He turned a page. “The Belgian. Filip. He is not just a cargo pilot. He is a collector. He sometimes brings things back. Samples. For the other side of the ledger.”

The other side. Not exports. Imports.

“What kind of samples?”

“The kind that require -70°C.” He finally looked at me, his eyes pale and watery, but sharp as flint. “You sent a message. A provocative one. It will force a response. But you are in the wrong city to see it. The response will not come from Casablanca. It will come from here. Rousseau does not dirty his own hands. He has a surgeon for that.”

A surgeon. The word hung in the damp air. The French surgeon in Rabat had carved my leg into a functional ruin. The memory was a phantom pain, sharper than the real one.

“Who?” I asked.

“A man who believes medicine has no borders. A man who works in a private clinic in Neuilly. A man who receives special deliveries at Orly.” He folded his newspaper neatly. “You have a ledger of what goes out. You should see the ledger of what comes in. It is… illustrative.”

He stood, left a few francs on the table for his drink, and walked away, melting into the stream of midday pedestrians.

I sat there, the drizzle gathering on the awning above me and dripping in a steady rhythm onto the pavement. I had come to Paris to follow a shipment, to understand the code. Instead, I had been given a new target. A surgeon. A man who imported samples that required a deep freeze.

The ledger in my bag was only half the story. It was the credit column. There was a debit column. A ledger of acquisitions. And if Rousseau’s network dealt in the cold currency of biology, then the imports would be the raw materials. The exports, the finished products.

My message to the villa would force Rousseau to act. To secure his supply line. To protect his surgeon.

I was no longer just a contaminant in his clean room.

I was a toxin in his bloodstream. And he would send his own white blood cells to hunt me. Not the man in the leather jacket. Something cleaner. More precise.

A surgeon.

I paid for my coffee and stood. The pain in my leg was a constant, grinding companion. I walked away from the café, into the Parisian rain, a ghost in a city of ghosts. I had a new name to find. A clinic in Neuilly. A ledger of things acquired.

The hunt had turned. The thing in the cage was awake, and it was not just growling. It was calling for a specialist. I had rattled the cage, and now the surgeon was being summoned, his instruments gleaming under sterile light, ready to perform an excision. To cut out the infection.

I had to find him first.



---

Chapter 18

CHAPTER 18

The clinic in Neuilly was a lie made of limestone and wrought iron. It sat behind a high wall on a quiet, tree-lined avenue, a place where money didn’t speak, it whispered. It whispered of discreet procedures, of rejuvenation, of maladies smoothed away without a trace. The brass plaque by the gate read Clinique du Parc. No names. Just an assurance of park-like serenity.

I had spent three days watching it from a rented room above a boulangerie two streets over. The view was a slice of the rear service entrance, a utilitarian mouth in the otherwise elegant facade. My leg, tuned to the damp Parisian cold, was a chorus of complaints. I ignored them. I had a pair of German binoculars, a notebook, and the slow, patient rhythm of surveillance. It was a kind of meditation. The pain was the mantra.

The ledger in my bag was useless here. This was the other side of Rousseau’s equation. The import side. The surgeon’s name was Dr. Anton Voss. Swiss. Trained in Zurich and Baltimore. A specialist in reconstructive and experimental immunology. His published work was dense, cautious, brilliant. It spoke of graft acceptance, of cellular tolerance. It did not speak of the things that might need grafting, or what the body might need to tolerate.

According to the brittle, chain-smoking clerk at the commercial registry I’d leaned on, Voss owned the clinic through a Liechtenstein holding company. The deliveries at Orly were invoiced as “specialized medical reagents” and “diagnostic cultures.” They were cleared under a diplomatic seal from a West African consulate whose ambassador was a frequent guest at Rousseau’s villa. The circuit was closed, elegant, and cold.

On the fourth morning, the rhythm broke.

A plain grey van, unmarked, rolled up to the service entrance just after dawn, when the street was still grey and empty. Two men got out. They weren’t orderlies. They moved with a tight, economical precision, scanning the street not with curiosity, but with a tactical assessment. One stayed by the van, a hand resting inside his open jacket. The other rang the bell. The door opened immediately, as if someone had been waiting behind it. A white-coated figure—too tall to be Voss—gestured. The man by the van opened the rear doors.

They unloaded a single object. A stainless steel cylinder, about the size of a small fire extinguisher, suspended in a custom frame with handles. It gleamed dully in the weak light. They carried it with exaggerated care, as if it were a religious relic or a bomb. The white-coated man led them inside. The door shut. The van drove away.

A liquid nitrogen Dewar. For transporting tissue. For keeping things very, very cold.

The thing in the cage was not just growling. It was being fed.

I left the binoculars on the sill. The hunt was no longer about observation. It was about penetration. I needed the other ledger. The one in Voss’s office, or his safe, or his mind.
  - Gaining entry required a different kind of uniform. At a friperie in the 10th, I found a suit of cheap, dark serge, a white shirt yellowed at the collar, and a thin black tie. I looked like a low-level functionary from a dying ministry. At a pharmacy, I bought a pair of wire-rimmed spectacles with plain glass lenses. The final piece was a leather-bound clipboard and a sheaf of official-looking forms I’d typed the night before on a borrowed machine, full of stilted bureaucratic French about municipal water pressure and pipe inspections for the arrondissement.

I presented myself at the service entrance at 2:17 p.m., the hour when post-lunch lethargy would be at its peak. I rang the bell. A porter in a green-striped apron opened the door, his face a mask of mild annoyance.

“Oui?”

“Inspection des eaux,” I said, my voice flattened by boredom. I held up the clipboard, tapping a mimeographed letterhead. “Routine check. Basements, boiler rooms. You have fifteen minutes to comply or we note a non-cooperation fee.”

The porter sighed, the universal sigh of a man confronted by the petty tyranny of the state. He waved me in. “Basement is through there, stairs at the end. Don’t bother the doctors. And be quick.”

The interior was all hushed corridors and pale green walls, smelling of antiseptic and floor wax. It was the smell of clean pain. I moved past a laundry cart, nodded to a nurse who didn’t look up from her charts, and found the stairwell. I went down.

The basement was a labyrinth of pipes, humming electrical panels, and storage rooms. I made a show of tapping pipes, scribbling nonsense on my form. I found the boiler room, a cathedral of heat and noise. And next to it, a heavy door with a new, shiny lock. A typed label on the wall read: Salle d’Archives – Accès Réservé.

I took a small set of picks from my inner pocket. The lock was a standard five-pin tumbler, well-made but not exotic. The noise of the boiler covered the subtle scrape of metal. The lock yielded after ninety seconds of focused pressure. I slipped inside, closing the door behind me.

It was not an archive.

The room was cold, a dry, artificial chill that immediately tightened the skin on my face and hands. The source was a large, industrial refrigeration unit humming in the corner. The walls were lined with steel shelves, holding boxes of surgical gloves, gauze, bottles of clear fluid. But in the center of the room, under a single, bright fluorescent light, stood a long stainless-steel table.

And on the table, connected to a nest of clear plastic tubing and a small, sighing pump, was the Dewar.

It was open.

Next to it, on a surgical tray, were three vials. They were small, glass, with black rubber stoppers. They sat in a metal rack. A label was typed on each. Not a name. A code.

S-117. Dakar. 12/03/62.

S-118. Bizerte. 01/04/63.

S-119. Casablanca. 05/05/63.

The dates were recent. The places were ports, former French military hubs. My mouth was dry. The cold air felt like a physical weight.

I heard the click of the door latch behind me.

I didn’t turn. My hand went to the inside of my jacket, to the grip of the Lebel revolver I’d carried since Casablanca.

“Please,” a calm, accented voice said. “Do not. It would be very messy. And we are in a clean room.”

I turned slowly.

Dr. Anton Voss stood in the doorway. He was smaller than his photographs suggested, a neat, compact man in his fifties with fine silver hair and eyes the colour of a winter lake. He wore a pristine white lab coat over a grey suit. His hands were bare, delicate, and very still. He held no weapon. He didn’t need to. The man who had been by the van that morning stood behind him, just in the corridor. His hand was inside his jacket.

“You are the water inspector,” Voss said. It wasn’t a question. His French was precise, Zurich-polished. “Your disguise is adequate for a porter. Not for me. We have been expecting a… curiosity. Since Rousseau’s message from the villa. He said you were persistent. A contaminant.”

I said nothing. My eyes went back to the vials. Casablanca. 05/05/63. Two months ago.

“You are looking at the future,” Voss said, stepping into the room. He closed the door, leaving his guard outside. He walked to the table, his movements economical. He did not look at me as a threat. He looked at me as a specimen. “Or rather, a branch of it. One that was pruned.”

“What are they?”

“Samples. Of a particular… instability. A side effect of certain prophylactic regimens administered to colonial forces in the late ‘50s. A triggered auto-immunity. The body turns on its own nervous system. It is slow. Inevitable. And until recently, untraceable to its source.” He picked up the Casablanca vial, held it to the light. “This one was a dockworker. He loaded crates for the Armée de l’Air in ‘58. He received a compulsory inoculation. He died screaming, unable to control his own diaphragm. His tissue tells the story.”

The cold in the room seeped into my bones. “Rousseau’s company made the serum.”

“Distributed it,” Voss corrected. “The formulation was developed by a military research unit in Algiers. Rousseau saw its commercial potential. A guaranteed market. Millions of doses. The side effect was noted, but the war was on. Such things are filed away. After the war, the files were… acquired. The samples are the proof. The leverage.”

“Blackmail,” I said. The word hung in the frigid air.

“A crude term. It is a negotiation. The French government is currently negotiating its future in Africa. Its military agreements. Its resource rights. These vials…” He gestured with the glass tube. “…are a powerful argument for generous terms. A reminder of past obligations. Of hidden debts.”

It was monstrous. It was perfect. It wasn’t about selling a cure. It was about weaponizing a hidden mistake, a colonial sin buried in the bloodstream of thousands. Rousseau wasn’t just a profiteer. He was an archaeologist of atrocity, digging up poison and calling it a commodity.

“And you?” I asked. “You’re just the curator?”

“I am the authenticator,” he said, placing the vial back in its rack with infinite care. “My work proves the link. The specific immunological signature. Without me, they are just vials of sick tissue. With me, they are a signed confession. Rousseau needs my signature.” He finally looked at me. “And you are a question mark. A variable. Rousseau believes you work for a rival syndicate. I am not so sure. You have the smell of a soloist. A ghost.”

“I’m the toxin,” I said.

“Yes. And the protocol for a toxin is isolation and expulsion.” He nodded toward the door. “Henri will take you somewhere quiet. You will disappear. The process will continue. The negotiations are delicate. They reach a critical point next week in Geneva. Your interference is not acceptable.”

It was the false victory. The shattering. I had thought I was hunting a corrupt businessman. I had walked into the heart of a silent, diplomatic war. The ledger of exports was a shopping list. This, the debit column, was a treaty written in poisoned blood. I had the truth. It was a truth that would bury me.

The door opened. Henri, the guard, entered. He had a pistol in his hand now, a sleek, black P38. He gestured with it. “Out. Back stairs.”

I moved, my leg stiff with cold and dread. Voss turned back to his table, to his vials of frozen history. I was already erased from his calculations.

Henri marched me down a narrow, concrete service stairwell and out a rear door into a small, walled courtyard where garbage bins stood. A delivery gate led to an alley. “Walk,” he said, the gun nudging my kidney. “To the black Citroën. Get in the back.”

This was the moment. The clean excision. I would be a body in the Seine, or a burnt-out car in the Bois de Boulogne. Another ghost.

We took three steps into the alley. The air was damp, smelling of rotting vegetables and wet stone.

A voice cut through the gloom from the alley’s mouth. “Arrêtez. Police.”

It was a woman’s voice. Clear. Authoritative.

Henri spun, bringing his pistol up. I dropped, my bad leg buckling, hitting the greasy cobbles.

The shot was not from Henri’s gun. It was a single, sharp crack. A rifle.

Henri’s head snapped back. He crumpled like a sack of wet cement. The P38 clattered on the stones.

I looked up.

A figure stepped from the shadow of a doorway. She wore a dark trench coat, her hair pulled back in a severe bun. She held a rifle with a shortened barrel, smoke wisping from the muzzle. It was the woman from the café in Paris. The one who had watched me. The one who had left the newspaper.

She lowered the rifle. Her eyes were on me, not on the dead man.

“Get up,” she said. Her French was native, but with no regional accent. It was the French of schools and statecraft. “We have approximately ninety seconds before the clinic’s security protocol activates.”

“Who are you?” I gasped, pushing myself up, my hand slipping in something wet that wasn’t water.

“Right now, I am your only exit.” She slung the rifle over her shoulder under her coat. “The question is not who I am. It is who you have been working for. And the answer, it seems, is no one. You are an amateur with a ledger. You have just stumbled into a state secret.”

“You’re not police.”

“I am a cleaner,” she said, moving to Henri’s body. With swift, practiced motions, she went through his pockets, retrieving a wallet, keys, the pistol. “For the Service. Rousseau’s operation is a known variable. A tolerated malignancy. You are an infection. You were supposed to be scared off. Instead, you broke into the operating theatre.” She stood, her gaze icy. “Dr. Voss is a protected asset. His work is a national asset. That,” she pointed a gloved finger at the clinic’s wall, “is the debit column for an empire. It stays balanced. It does not get spilled in the street by a limping privateer.”

The colonial aftermath. It wasn’t just a setting. It was the crime scene. And I was the fool trampling the evidence.

“They’re blackmailing the government with its own crimes,” I said.

“They are stabilizing the government,” she corrected, her voice a razor. “With the tools it left behind. It is ugly. It is necessary. And you are going to forget it. You are going to take your ledger and your moral outrage and you are going to leave Paris. Tonight.”

“Or?”

“Or the next shot is not for your guard.” She stepped closer. “You think you uncovered something? You saw the bait. The real trap is around you. It has been since Casablanca. Rousseau let you follow the shipments. He let you come to Paris. He fed you Voss. Because a man who is chasing a surgeon is not looking at the man holding the scalpel. You were meant to come here. You were meant to be erased here. A tidy end.”

The false victory shattered, completely. My pursuit, my cunning, my slow unraveling of the code—it had all been a script. I was a mouse in a maze, thinking I was solving the puzzle, while the lab technicians watched and took notes.

The sound of a raised voice came from inside the clinic. An alarm began to bleat, muted by the walls.

“Time’s up,” she said. “The alley ends on Rue des Acacias. Turn left. Keep walking. Do not run. A car will pick you up in three blocks. It will take you to Gare de Lyon. You will be on the 19:15 to Marseille. You will board a freighter there. Your name is on the manifest.”

“And if I don’t?”

“Then you die a patriot,” she said, without a hint of irony. “Shot by a rogue element of a foreign syndicate while trying to expose a terrible crime. A small, noble paragraph in Le Monde. And the balance is maintained.”

She melted back into the doorway’s shadow and was gone.

I stood over Henri’s body, the clinic’s alarm growing more urgent. The truth was not a weapon. It was a burden. And it belonged to the state. I was a temporary, inconvenient holder. The ledger in my bag was not evidence. It was my death warrant, with a travel clause.

I turned and walked down the alley, my leg screaming with every step. I did not run. A ghost, following orders. The toxin, identified and neutralized.

The black Citroën was there, just as she said. The back door opened. I got in. It smelled of Gauloises and old leather. The driver didn’t speak. He pulled into the traffic, smooth and anonymous.

I looked back at the wall of the clinic, receding in the rain-streaked window. Voss was in there, with his vials and his cold, clean truth. Rousseau was in his villa, a spider at the center of a web of silent, brutal leverage. And I was in the back of a car, being escorted to the edge of the board.

The hunt had turned, once. Now it had been ended. By a higher power. A colder logic.

The car slid through the Parisian streets, a coffin on wheels. I had come to find a surgeon. I had found the surgery. And I was the tumor, neatly removed.
  - Gare de Lyon was a cathedral of noise and steam and hurried goodbyes. The 19:15 to Marseille was boarding. A ticket was in my hand, first class, compartment 7B. My name was not on it. The name was “J. Lenoir.”

I stood on the platform, the smell of coal smoke and damp wool thick in the air. My bag felt heavier than it ever had. It held the ledger, my clothes, the ghost of my purpose. The driver had given me a final, wordless look before driving away. The message was clear: the track or the morgue.

I believed the woman. The Service. She had the eyes of someone who managed catastrophes for a living. I was a small one. A containable one.

I found compartment 7B. It was empty. I stowed my bag, sat by the window. The platform was a blur of faces, none of them looking for me. I was already a memory.

The train gave a jolt and began to move, slowly, with a great exhalation of steam. Paris began to slide past the window—the backs of buildings, washing lines, the distant glow of the city under the evening gloom.

I had the false victory. I had seen the monster’s heart. And I had been shown my place: a bystander. A casualty of a deeper, older war that was fought in boardrooms and clinics, with ledgers and liquid nitrogen. The colonial aftermath wasn’t a landscape. It was an ongoing operation. A body still twitching on the table, with men like Voss monitoring its vital signs, and men like Rousseau selling the prognosis.

The train picked up speed, clattering over points. The city fell away, replaced by the darkened suburbs, then open countryside swallowed by the night.

I closed my eyes. The grind of the wheels on the tracks was the same rhythm as the pain in my leg. A constant. A companion.

I must have slept. The jolt of the train stopping woke me. We were not in a station. We were on a dark stretch of track, somewhere in the countryside. The compartment light was off. The corridor was silent.

Then my door slid open.

A conductor stood there, his cap pulled low. But he was too broad, too still. It wasn’t the conductor.

It was the man from the villa. The one in the leather jacket. His face was a shadow under the cap’s brim. In his hand was not a ticket punch. It was a long, thin blade, like a surgical scalpel, but longer.

He stepped inside and closed the door behind him.

“The Service cleans with bullets,” he said, his voice a low rasp. “Rousseau cleans with a knife. He prefers it. More personal.”

The car was motionless. No sounds of other passengers. We were alone, in a dark capsule between nowhere and nowhere.

The false victory was over. The shattering was complete. The cleaner had cleaned, but the surgeon had sent his own instrument. A precise, final cut.

The man in the leather jacket raised the blade. It caught a sliver of moonlight from the window.

The hunt hadn’t ended. It had just left the city. And in the dark, away from the state’s clean-up crews, the old rules applied. Blood, and steel, and a ledger that someone still wanted back.

The train gave a shudder, but did not move. We were stuck there, in the silent, French dark.

And the blade came forward.



---

Chapter 19

CHAPTER 19

The blade came forward, not in a lunge, but in a slow, deliberate push. It was a probe, not a strike. The man in the leather jacket was a professional. He wasn’t here for theater. He was here for a specific, contained result. The scalpel-like tool was an extension of that philosophy. Rousseau’s philosophy. A cleaner’s tool for a cleaner’s work.

I didn’t move from the seat. Moving was what he wanted. It would create the opening for the definitive, clinical entry. The pain in my leg was a white-hot coal, but it was also a tether, a grounding wire. It told me where my body ended and the world began. The world, at that moment, was this dark compartment, the smell of stale tobacco and old upholstery, and the man with the surgeon’s instrument.

I let my right hand, resting on my thigh, go slack. My left arm was braced against the window frame, holding my weight against the jolt that had woken me.

“The ledger is gone,” I said. My voice was rough with sleep, which was good. It sounded like resignation. “Voss has it. Or the Service has it. Your employer is tidying an empty room.”

The blade stopped, six inches from my sternum. The man’s face was still in shadow, but I saw the tilt of his head. The cap’s brim dipped slightly. “Rousseau does not tidy. He excises. The ledger was the symptom. You are the infection.”

So that was it. Not retrieval. Not interrogation. Excision. I was a loose thread, and Rousseau’s method was to burn the end, not snip it. The train’s silent immobility made sense now. This stretch of track had been chosen. The other passengers, if there ever were any in this car, had been moved or warned. We were in a surgical theatre, and the table was a first-class compartment on the overnight to nowhere.

The train shuddered again, a metallic groan deep in its belly. It was the sound of pressure being released, of brakes held too long. The man’s balance shifted, a micro-adjustment of his hips. It was the only opening I would get.

I dropped my right hand from my thigh, not toward the blade, but down beside the seat, to the floor. My fingers closed around the neck of the empty wine bottle the conductor had left hours earlier. I swung it up and across in a short, tight arc, not aiming for his head, but for the blade-hand.

The bottle connected with a dull, sickening crack against his wrist. It wasn’t glass on bone; it was glass on the steel of a watch casing. But the force was enough. The blade flicked sideways, scoring a long, shallow line across the front of my coat before it clattered to the floor. He grunted, more in annoyance than pain, and his left hand came up in a fast, hard chop aimed at my throat.

I was already moving, leveraging off the window frame with my bad leg screaming in protest. I threw myself forward, not away, but into him. My shoulder caught him in the midsection. He was solid, like hitting a sack of wet sand. We went down together, a tangle of limbs and the smell of leather and cheap train soap. The compartment was too small for a proper fight. It was a contest of angles and pressure and who could absorb the most punishment against the hard edges of the furniture.

He was stronger. Younger. His hands found my throat, thumbs pressing into the hollow beneath my Adam’s apple. The world began to narrow, the edges of my vision fuzzing into a dark grey static. I drove a knee up, but it was weak, glancing off his hip. My left hand scrabbled at the floor, searching for the blade, finding only grit and the torn edge of the carpet.

My right hand, still clutching the bottle by the neck, flailed back and connected with the compartment door. Not the wood, but the metal handle. I hooked it. Pulled.

The door slid open with a crash. We spilled out into the dark corridor, a single, struggling mass. The sudden space changed the calculus. He lost his leverage for a moment, his grip on my throat loosening as he braced to stop the roll. I sucked in a ragged, burning breath and brought the bottle down on the side of his head.

This time, the glass broke.

He roared, a short, animal sound, and rolled off me. Shards of green glass skittered across the corridor floor. I scrambled back, pushing myself up against the opposite wall. The pain in my leg was a nova, blinding and absolute. He was already rising, a dark shape blotting out the faint moonlight from the compartment window. A trickle of blackness ran down his temple. He wiped at it, looked at his gloved hand, then at me.

He didn’t speak. He just came forward again, more carefully now, his stance wider, his hands open. The blade was gone, but his hands were the real weapons. He would finish it the old-fashioned way. Efficient. Personal.

I pushed myself upright, my back against the cold window of the corridor. We were alone in the silent car. The only sounds were our breathing and the distant, mournful sigh of wind across the fields.

“He’s cutting his losses, your surgeon,” I gasped, the words raw. “You’re the last stitch in a wound he’s already sealed. What does he pay for that?”

“A clean conscience,” the man rasped. His voice was closer to a whisper now, strained. “And a pension.”

He feinted with his left, then drove his right fist toward my solar plexus. I twisted, taking the blow on my ribs instead. The air left me in a whoosh. I grabbed at the arm as it retracted, pulling him off balance, and drove my forehead into the bridge of his nose. There was a wet crunch. He staggered back, and I let him go, stumbling toward the end of the car, toward the door that led to the next carriage.

My vision swam. Each breath was a knife in my side. I fumbled with the heavy door handle, my fingers slick with sweat or blood, I couldn’t tell. Behind me, I heard him spit, a thick, wet sound, and then his footsteps, steady and relentless.

The door gave way. I fell through into the violent, roaring cold of the coupling space. The wind tore at my coat, screaming through the metal grille underfoot. The next car was a dark silhouette, ten feet away across a yawning gap of rushing ties and gravel. The train was a dead snake, but the night was alive, howling through its ribs.

I looked back. He stood in the doorway, a solid block of shadow. He watched me, calculating the jump. He would make it. I wasn’t sure I could.

To jump was to play his game, on his terms, in a dark field where he could hunt at his leisure. It was the logic of the prey.

I turned away from the next car. Instead, I looked down, through the grille, at the ground rushing past in a dark blur. Too fast to jump. A sure way to break an ankle, or a neck.

The footsteps started again behind me. He was coming.

I dropped to my knees on the grille, the metal biting through the fabric of my trousers. I gripped the cold handrail with both hands, and before I could think, before the animal fear could lock my muscles, I swung my legs out and down, into the black rush beneath the train.

For a second, I hung there, suspended between the shuddering carriages, the wind screaming up at me, the gravel and ties a grey streak inches below my boots. My arms screamed in their sockets. My bad leg was a column of pure, white agony.

I looked up. His face appeared over the edge of the coupling platform, a pale oval. He saw what I was doing. Hanging, not jumping. Disappearing under the train.

His hand shot down to grab my wrist. I let go with my right hand, swinging wildly, and grabbed for the undercarriage. My fingers closed around a cold, greasy strut. I kicked out with my feet, finding a purchase on some other piece of framing. I was now clinging to the guts of the train, hidden in the narrow, mechanical space between the wheels.

Above, I heard him curse. He couldn’t follow. Not without dropping down and risking being crushed if the train decided to move. He could wait, but waiting was a form of losing. The operation was meant to be quick, clean. A body in a compartment, easily explained. A body under a train was messy. It raised questions.

I didn’t move. I just held on, my face pressed against cold, oily metal, breathing in the stink of grease and damp earth. The wind was less here, but the cold was deeper, seeping up from the ground. I listened.

For a long time, there was nothing but the wind and the creak of the stationary train. Then, a final, soft sound from above. A boot on the grille, stepping back. Then the slam of the heavy door.

He was gone. For now.

I waited another five minutes, my muscles trembling with the strain. Then, inch by inch, I pulled myself along the undercarriage, moving toward the rear of the train, away from the car where he would be waiting. I found a set of steps leading up to a maintenance platform at the very back of the last carriage. I hauled myself up, every movement a fresh fire in my side and leg, and collapsed onto the small, open platform.

I lay there for a time, staring up at the cold spray of stars. The silence was immense. No city hum, no human sound. Just the vast, indifferent dark of the French countryside. I was a piece of refuse that had fallen from the undercarriage of the colonial machine, left to rust in a field.

Eventually, I sat up. My coat was torn, my ribs ached with each breath, and the cut across my chest from his blade was a stinging line of heat. But I was alive. The cleaner’s instrument had failed to make the cut.

I checked my pockets. My papers were still there. My money. The train ticket, now useless. I had nothing else. No weapon. No plan. Just the clothes on my back and the persistent, grinding pain that was my constant companion.

I looked back down the length of the train. It was a string of darkened, silent boxes, a monument to interrupted journeys. In one of those boxes, a man with a broken nose was waiting, or was already reporting his failure on a railway telephone. Rousseau would know within the hour.

I couldn’t stay here. When the train was cleared, they would find me. Either the railway police or Rousseau’s man, coming back for a second, more thorough attempt.

I climbed down from the platform, my legs buckling as I hit the gravel embankment. I stood in the ditch beside the track, getting my bearings. The land was flat, featureless. To the east, the sky held the faintest suggestion of a lighter grey. Dawn was hours away, but the night was beginning to thin.

A mile or so to the north, I saw a cluster of darker shapes against the gloom. A farm, or a small village. A place with a telephone. A place where a man with torn clothes and a limp might buy a coat, a meal, and a few hours of anonymity.
