I started walking, leaving the dead train on its tracks. Each step was a negotiation with the pain. The rhythm of my walking was no longer the same as the grind of the wheels. It was slower, more deliberate. A man-made rhythm. A choice.

The hunt had left the city. It had come to the countryside, to the dark spaces between stations. But the rules had shifted again. I was no longer just the quarry. I was a loose end that had proven difficult to trim. And loose ends have a way of snagging on things, of unraveling other, carefully woven parts of the tapestry.

Rousseau wanted me excised to clean the ledger’s final page. But the ledger was with Voss now, in the hands of the state. My death wouldn’t erase it. It would just be another entry. A curiosity. A man killed on a train. Why?

As I walked, the cold air clearing the last of the fog from my head, a new, colder thought took shape. Rousseau wasn’t just cleaning up. He was erasing a witness to his own failure. The ledger was gone, yes. But I had seen it. I had held it. I knew the names, the dates, the cold arithmetic of betrayal. I knew Rousseau’s signature methodology—the clinical, personal cut.

And I knew where he lived.

The villa outside Marseille. The operating theatre with the sea view.

He had sent his instrument to silence me. But instruments can be turned. They can be taken from the surgeon’s hand and used to make an incision of one’s own. The colonial aftermath wasn’t just an operation on a twitching body. It was a war of scars. Each man carried his own, and the deepest ones were not always visible.

The lights of the village grew closer, pinpricks in the vast dark. Shelter. A telephone. A chance to stop reacting and start cutting back.

But first, I had to get there. And every step was a mile. Every shadow beside the road was a man in a leather jacket, waiting. The night was no longer an ally. It was the medium in which we both now moved, hunter and hunted, surgeon and infection, two ghosts of a dying regime, playing out the last bloody stitches of its end.



---

Chapter 20

CHAPTER 20

The village was called Saint-Martin-de-la-Brume, or so the rusted sign, pocked with old bullet holes, claimed. It was less a village than a clot of stone buildings huddled in a shallow valley, as if trying to hide from the sky. The lights were not welcoming. They were sparse, yellowish, and tremulous behind thick glass, like the eyes of a wary animal. My approach was not stealthy. It was the slow, grinding advance of a damaged thing, each footfall a deliberate concession to gravity and pain. The road had given way to a gravel track, and the gravel was a minefield of shifting angles. My bad leg had ceased to be a separate entity. It was the hot, pulsing core of my existence, and the rest of me was merely its aching satellite.

The first building was a barn, its great doors sagging on iron hinges. The smell of old hay and animal dung seeped out, a pastoral ghost. Past it, the main street was a strip of mud and cobble, barely ten yards long. A single café, its interior dark. A boulangerie with a closed shutter. A post office with a tricolor flag hanging limp and wet. The only sign of life was a thin curl of smoke from a chimney at the far end, and a faint, tinny sound of radio static leaking from a cracked window.

Shelter. A telephone. The two ideas had kept me moving for the last hour. Now, faced with the reality of this place, they seemed like childish fantasies. This was not a hub. This was an endpoint. The kind of place where a stranger knocking after midnight would be met with a shotgun, not a welcome. The colonial aftermath had many faces: the frantic, paranoid energy of Casablanca, the calcified resentment of Marseille’s vieux port, and this—the deep, exhausted silence of the French countryside that had been bled dry by wars it never understood. I was just another piece of fallout, washed up on its doorstep.

I chose the house with the smoke. It was slightly larger than the others, with a low stone wall enclosing a patch of garden gone to winter rot. The path to the door was made of uneven slate. I focused on them, counting each slab as a minor victory. Seven. Eight. Nine.

Before I could raise my hand to knock, the door opened.

A woman stood there, backlit by the low glow of an oil lamp. She was in her sixties, perhaps, with a face that was all severe angles and deep lines, as if carved from the same granite as the hills. She wore a heavy woolen shawl over a dark dress. Her eyes were not frightened. They were assessing, flinty, moving from my mud-caked shoes up to my face, lingering on the sweat and grime and the tension I could not hide. She held no shotgun. Her hands, rough and red-knuckled, were empty at her sides.

“You’re lost,” she said. It wasn’t a question. Her voice was the sound of gravel under a boot.

“My car broke down,” I said, the lie automatic, worn smooth from use. “On the road from Arles. I need to use a telephone.”

She stared at me for a long moment. The radio static hissed behind her. It was a news broadcast, the words too distorted by interference to make out. “There is no telephone here that works for strangers,” she said. “The post office line is for the mayor. He is in Avignon.”

“Then shelter. Just for an hour. To get off this leg.” I let a fraction of the pain show in my voice. It wasn’t difficult.

Her eyes flicked down to my left leg, then back to my face. She was reading the story of the limp, the careful distribution of weight. She had seen men come home broken before. “The café has a room. Madame Bisset lets it.”

“It’s dark.”

“She is dead. Two years.” The woman shifted her weight, blocking more of the doorway. “You are not from Arles.”

“No.”

“You have the look of the north. Or further. The rain there is different. Softer.”

“It’s all wet,” I said.

A ghost of something passed over her face. Not a smile. A recognition. She stepped back. “One hour. You sit by the fire. You do not speak. The hour passes, you leave. D’accord?”

“D’accord.”

The room was small, low-ceilinged, dominated by a massive stone fireplace where a few logs smoldered. The heat was a physical force, almost shocking after the miles of cold dark. It made the sweat on my back go icy. The furnishings were sparse, sturdy, old. A crucifix hung on one wall. A faded photograph of a young man in a uniform—the Great War, by the look of it—sat on the mantel. The air smelled of woodsmoke, soup, and a faint, sharp odor of camphor.

“Sit,” she said, pointing to a wooden chair with a worn cushion. I lowered myself into it, a slow, controlled collapse. The relief was immediate and profound, a wave that left me lightheaded. I leaned my cane against the hearth.

She did not offer me food or drink. She took a seat in a rocking chair on the other side of the fire, picked up a darning needle and a sock, and began to work. The radio continued to mutter. The clock on the wall ticked with a heavy, ponderous rhythm. She did not look at me again. We sat in a silence that was not companionable, but contractual.

I watched the flames. The hunt had left the city. It had come to the countryside. Rousseau’s instrument was out there, in the vast dark between stations. A man who moved like a stiletto. He would not be flustered by the missed opportunity on the train. He would be recalibrating. Considering the terrain. A wounded man moves toward shelter, toward light. He would know this. He would be considering the same map, the same sparse points of reference.

The woman’s voice cut through the static. “They are looking for someone. On the radio. A man who attacked a conductor on the Paris-Marseille line. A madman. Dangerous.”

I kept my eyes on the fire. “Is that so.”

“They say he is likely armed. That he may be in this region.”

“A lot of country out there.”

“Not so much,” she said, her needle moving in and out of the wool. “Not so many places to go. For a man who does not know the land.”

I turned my head to look at her. Her face was bent over her work, the firelight carving deep shadows in the lines around her mouth. “You think I am this madman?”

“I think a man who is running from a broken car does not have eyes like yours.” She glanced up, her gaze needle-sharp. “You are listening for footsteps. Not on the road. In your own head.”

She was frighteningly perceptive. A lifetime of hard living in an isolated place had honed a different kind of intelligence. She could read weather in the clouds and trouble in a man’s face. I said nothing.

“My husband had those eyes,” she said, nodding toward the photograph. “After Verdun. He came back, but he was always listening for the shells. Always waiting for the next attack. He died in his sleep, twenty years later. But he was killed at Verdun. The rest was just… paperwork.”

The colonial aftermath. It wasn’t just in the cities, in the shifting allegiances and the burning of ledgers. It was here, in this quiet room, in the memory of a war that had bled into the next one, and the next. A legacy of waiting for the next attack.

“I am not a madman,” I said, my voice low. “I am a loose end.”

She considered the phrase, her needle pausing. “A thread that has been cut.”

“Not cut cleanly. They want to trim it.”

“And you do not wish to be trimmed.”

“No.”

She rocked slowly, the chair creaking in time with the clock. “The post office telephone does not work for strangers. But there is another. At the gendarmerie. Four kilometers east. The brigadier there, he has a direct line. He is also the mayor’s cousin.”

A trap or a test. Possibly both. “I don’t need the gendarmerie.”

“Of course not,” she said, as if it were obvious. She went back to her darning. The hour was slipping away. The fire was dying down. My leg, in the warmth, had stiffened into a solid, throbbing mass. Getting up would be a fresh kind of agony.

“Why did you let me in?” I asked.

This time she did look at me, and her eyes were flat, devoid of pity. “Because the man you are running from will not knock. He will not sit by the fire. He will come through the window or the back door. And I would rather have you here, between him and my door, than have him find me alone.”

It was the most honest thing anyone had said to me in weeks. I almost smiled. “You are using me as a barricade.”

“You are a barricade that needs a fire and a chair,” she said. “It is a fair trade.”

The radio crackled, the signal fading into a wash of noise, then surging back. A different voice now, clear and official. “…repeating, an all-points alert. The individual may be attempting to cross into the Alpes-Maritimes. Considered extremely dangerous. Do not approach. Notify authorities immediately.”

The woman’s eyes met mine over the fire. The Alpes-Maritimes. The Italian border. They were herding me, shaping the narrative. A madman fleeing justice, making for the frontier. It was cleaner than the truth. It had a simple, digestible geometry.

“Your hour is nearly up,” she said.

I pushed myself up, using the chair arm and then my cane. The pain was a bright, white flare that receded into a deep, familiar ache. I was a machine of pain, fueled by it. “Thank you for the fire.”

“The track behind the barn,” she said, not looking up from her darning. “It goes south, through the woods. It is used by woodcutters. It is not on any map. It comes out near the river, five kilometers from here. There is a ferryman, if he is not drunk. He will take you across for fifty francs. The other side is not this département.”

I stood still, absorbing this. “Why tell me?”

“A barricade is no use if it is surrounded in the open,” she said. “It needs a line of retreat. Go.”

I went. Out into the biting cold, which felt like a slap after the close heat of the room. The night had grown darker, the cloud cover complete, smothering the moon and stars. I found the track behind the barn, a thin, muddy gash disappearing into a wall of pine. I entered the woods.

The silence here was different. It was porous, filled with the drip of water from needles, the scuttle of some small creature in the underbrush, the sigh of wind in the high branches. I moved as quietly as I could, but my progress was a symphony of crushed leaves, labored breath, and the soft thud-tap of my cane. The path was steep in places, slick with mud and pine needles. I fell once, my bad leg buckling on a hidden root. I went down hard on my side, the impact driving the air from my lungs. I lay there for a moment, cheek against the cold, wet earth, smelling rot and fungus. The temptation to stay down, to let the cold and the dark take over, was a tangible thing, a warm whisper in my ear. It would be easy. An end to the negotiation.

I thought of the villa outside Marseille. The operating theatre with the sea view. Rousseau, in his sterile sanctuary, directing operations with a surgeon’s remove. He had turned my life into a clinical exercise. A problem of excision. I pushed myself up, mud-slick and shaking. My hands were numb. No. The ledger was gone. But I had seen it. I was the last witness to his precise, personal betrayals. My death wouldn’t be an entry in a state’s ledger. It would be the final stitch in his work. And I decided, there in the mud, that I would not be his perfect closure. I would be the snag. The infection. I would make a cut of my own.

It took me over two hours to cover the five kilometers. The trees eventually thinned, and the sound of water grew from a murmur to a low, steady roar. I emerged onto a rocky bank beside a wide, fast-moving river, black as oil under the night sky. A small, dilapidated shack stood a little way upstream, a faint glow in its window.

The ferryman was drunk. He was a huge, bearded man in a stained peacoat, smelling of wine and river mud. He sat on an upturned barrel inside his shack, a bottle of vin ordinaire at his feet. A single lantern hung from a beam, swinging gently.

“Fifty francs,” he grunted, before I could speak. “In advance. No night crossing without advance.”

I peeled the notes from my damp wallet. He snatched them, held them close to the lantern, then stuffed them into his pocket. “Boat’s outside. You row. My arms are tired.”

“You’re the ferryman.”

“And I’m tired. You want across, you provide the labor. Consider it a discount.”

The boat was a flat-bottomed punt, half-full of icy water. I bailed it out with a rusted can while he watched from the doorway, taking pulls from his bottle. When it was merely damp, I used my cane to steady myself and climbed in. The ferryman untied the rope from a stone post and gave the boat a shove that sent it spinning into the current.

“The pole!” he barked, pointing to a long wooden shaft. “Use the pole, you idiot. Or you’ll end up in the Mediterranean.”

I wrestled with the pole, jamming it into the riverbed. The current was strong, greedy. It wanted to take the boat and me with it. For several minutes, it was a brutal, silent struggle—me leaning into the pole, muscles screaming, the boat slewing sideways, the black water churning. Slowly, incrementally, I gained the far side. The opposite bank was steeper, lined with willows. I managed to hook a branch and pull the boat in.

As I climbed out, my legs trembling with fatigue, the ferryman’s voice carried across the water, loud and slurred. “You! The man on the radio. The madman. That’s you, eh?”

I froze, one hand on a willow trunk.

He laughed, a wet, ragged sound. “Don’t worry. I don’t care. The radio is full of lies. But you should know. A car came to the village. Just after you left. A dark car. One man. He went to the house with the smoke. He is not there now.”

He had spoken to the woman. He had found my trail. He was hours behind, but he was a hunter who knew how to read signs. And now he knew the direction.

The ferryman took another drink. “He paid a hundred francs for the same information I gave you. He did not look tired. He did not need to row.”

He gave another shove of laughter, then turned and stumbled back into his shack.

I stood on the bank, the water roaring in my ears. The other side was not this département. But it was not sanctuary. It was just another piece of the board. Rousseau’s instrument was across the river now. The distance between us was not geography. It was time, and pain, and stamina. He had all the advantages.

Except one.

He was following me. And I, now, knew he was there.

I turned away from the river and began to climb the bank. The woods here were different, older, with great gnarled oaks and thickets of bramble. I had no path. I had no destination. I had only the cold calculus of the hunted: to find a place where the ground favored the wounded over the swift. A place where a loose end could snag, and twist, and prepare to cut the hand that sought to grasp it.

The night was absolute. The hunter was in it with me. We were two ghosts of a dying regime, playing out the last bloody stitches. But the needle was in my hand now. And I knew how to make it hurt.



---

Chapter 21

CHAPTER 21

The woods were a cathedral of rot and silence. The great oaks, their branches arthritic against the starless vault, held a damp quiet that swallowed sound. My own breathing was too loud, a ragged bellows in the stillness. The river’s roar had faded to a murmur, then to nothing. Here, the only water was the drip from last week’s rain, falling from leaf to moss to black earth. My leg was past pain. It was a geological fact, a tectonic plate of agony that shifted with each step, grinding bone against scar tissue. The cold was a mercy. It numbed the edges, turning the fire in my knee to a deep, glacial ache.

I moved not with purpose, but with a predator’s idle patience. A fox would move this way, or a badger—not fleeing, but melting into the texture of the land. The ground was a quilt of dead leaves over soft loam. It took an impression. My own boots left pits that seemed to shout. Rousseau’s man, if he was as good as his reputation, would read them like headlines. Limping. Tired. Heading east, away from the road. I needed to change the story.

I found a stream, a thin silver trickle over moss-slick stones. The water was knife-blade cold. I stepped into it, the shock traveling up my spine. I went upstream, against the feeble current, for two hundred paces. The stones were unstable, but they left no track. At a place where a fallen birch created a bridge, I climbed out, careful to leave no mud on the bark. I struck north now, perpendicular to my original line, moving from the cover of one massive trunk to the next.

The calculus was simple, born of a hundred nights in the bled, waiting for contacts who never came. He had speed. He had endurance. He had the certainty of the hunter. I had the cunning of the cornered rat. My advantage was the needle in my hand: the knowledge of his pursuit. I could not outrun him. So I would make the run cost him.

An hour into the woods, I found the place.

It was a shepherd’s shelter, long abandoned. The stone walls had slumped in on themselves, and the sod roof had collapsed, birthing a thicket of nettle and wild raspberry from its gut. It stood in a slight depression, flanked by two lightning-blasted pines. The ground before it was open, a bowl of wet grass and mud. A terrible place to hide. A perfect place to wait.

I circled it at a distance, a tightening coil. No fresh sign. Only the old, sour smell of decayed wool and animal dung. I approached from the rear, where the wall had fully given way. Inside was a tomb of shadows and the sweet, cloying scent of fungus. I did not enter. I moved to the side of the doorway, where the wall was still chest-high. I sank down into a nest of ferns, my back against the cold stone. The position gave me a clear line of sight across the bowl to the tree line from which I had just come. The ferns would break my outline. The stone would stop a bullet.

Then, I waited. Waiting is not passive. It is an act of compression. You fold the noise of your mind down, down, until it is a diamond of pure attention. You listen not for sounds, but for the absence of them. You watch not for movement, but for the hesitation of the night. The drip of water became a metronome. The sigh of wind in the high pines became a breath. My own body receded. The pain in my leg was a distant signal, a telegraph click from a forgotten front.

He came with the first grey smear of false dawn.

He did not come through the tree line I had watched. He came from the west, skirting the edge of the depression, using the deeper shadow of the pines. He was a silhouette first, then a man as he paused at the tree line. He was tall, lean. He wore a dark overcoat, unbuttoned. He held himself utterly still. He was studying the shepherd’s hut.

He was good. He had not followed my stream trick. He had cut my original trail, lost it, and deduced a logical destination. A place of shelter for a wounded man. He saw the open ground and knew its danger. He would not cross it.

For a long minute, he was a statue. Then, he moved. Not forward, but laterally, beginning a slow, patient circuit of the depression, keeping to the trees. He was going to flank the hut, approach from the blind side. He was methodical. Unhurried. This was his profession.

I let him complete half his circuit. Let him commit to the tree line on the far side. Let his focus settle entirely on the dark maw of the collapsed roof.

I spoke, my voice low, but carrying in the bowl of damp air.

“The ground is softer over here.”

He froze. His head did not snap toward me. It turned, slowly, a machine calibrating. He was twenty yards away, a grey shape in the gloom. I saw the pale oval of his face.

“You are making this difficult,” he said. His French was Parisian, clean and without accent. An administrator’s voice.

“I am a difficult man,” I said.

“You are a tired man. In pain. You left a trail a child could follow.”

“And yet you are over there, and I am over here.”

A faint smile might have touched his mouth. It was hard to tell. “A momentary correction. You have no weapon. I do.”

“You have a pistol in your coat. Right side. You are left-handed. You keep the flap unbuttoned. Efficient.”

His stillness deepened. I had taken a small thing from him. The hunter dislikes being seen.

“Rousseau wants you alive,” he said. It was not a threat. It was a piece of information, laid between us like a card.

“Rousseau wants the list. He thinks I have it. Do I look like a man carrying a list?”

“You look like a man who knows where it is.”

“I look like a man who needs a surgeon and a bottle of brandy. The list is a ghost. Rousseau is chasing a draft from a closed window.”

He took a single step forward, out of the deepest shadow. The grey light caught the angles of his face. He was younger than I expected, perhaps forty, with the calm, empty eyes of a man who has reconciled himself to a dirty business. “Then why run?”

“Because men like you do not ask questions. You administer solutions.”

He acknowledged this with a slight tilt of his head. “You could have taken the boat across the river. You could have found a road, tried for a town. Instead, you came here. To this ruin. You wanted to be found.”

“I wanted to see what was following me. I am not disappointed. You are very professional.”

“Flattery is a last resort.”

“Observation,” I said. “You work for the Service. But not directly. You are a pigiste. A contractor. Cleaner work for a cleaner conscience. Rousseau hires you when the stitches need to be invisible.”

He said nothing. His silence was confirmation.

“He will not pay you if I am dead,” I said.

“He will not pay me if I fail.”

“Then we are at an impasse.”

“No,” he said. “I have the gun.”

He moved then. Not for his pistol, but sideways, flowing behind the thicker trunk of a pine. He broke my line of sight. I pressed myself flatter against the stone. He was re-positioning. The conversation was over. It had only ever been a tool, a way to fix my location, to measure the distance.

A minute passed. Two. The light was leaching into the woods, turning black to grey, grey to green. The details of the world were emerging, sharp and cruel. I could see the individual fronds of the fern before my face. I could see the worm trails in the mud of the depression.

A magpie chattered, far off. Then silence again.

He was gone. Or he was everywhere.

The tension in a hunt is a wire. It can be drawn tight, singing, until it snaps. Or it can be let go slack, until you forget it is there. He was trying for the latter. He wanted the waiting to work on me. To let the pain and the cold do their slow, persuasive work.

I closed my eyes. I listened with my skin.

The sound, when it came, was not from the trees. It was from the hut itself. A faint, gritty scrape of stone on stone. From inside.

He had not circled. He had doubled back. He had gone in through the collapsed rear, moving through the ruin while my attention was on the tree line. He was now inside the shelter, separated from me by three feet of crumbling wall. He had outmaneuvered me. The needle was in my hand, but he had guided it away from the vein.

I held my breath. My mind was a white, blank page. There was no plan. There was only the animal instinct of the trapped.

I heard his own breath, a controlled, quiet intake. He was listening for mine.

With a groan that came from my gut and my ruined leg, I pushed myself up. I made it loud. I made it the sound of a man breaking cover, making a desperate, clumsy run for it. I stumbled forward, away from the wall, into the open mouth of the depression.

The shot was instantaneous. It was not from inside the hut. It came from the tree line to the north. A sharp, flat crack that split the dawn. Chips of stone exploded from the wall where my head had been.

There were two of them.

The one in the tree line was the shooter. The professional in the hut was the bait, the anvil. I had walked onto it. The conversation, the flanking maneuver—all of it theater to drive me toward the hidden gun.

I was already moving, not away, but toward the hut. It was the only piece of cover. The second shot came, kicking up a gout of mud at my heels. Then I was at the doorway, plunging into the fungal stink and the darkness.

Inside was a chaos of fallen beams and slumped stone. A figure straightened from behind a broken lintel. The professional. His pistol was in his left hand, coming up. There was no room for finesse. I was already falling, my bad leg giving way, not in pretense but in sheer, catastrophic failure. I fell into him, a tangle of limbs and momentum. We went down in a crush of rotten wood and wet stone.

He was strong, and he was trained. He tried to roll, to get his arm free for a shot. My hand found his wrist. I slammed it down against a jagged piece of masonry. Once. Twice. He grunted, his fingers spasming. The pistol clattered away into the debris.

His knee drove into my bad leg.

White light. A silent scream that locked my jaw. The world dissolved into nauseating brilliance. He scrambled free, kicking back from me. He was going for the gun.

From outside, a shout. “Sortez!” Get out. The shooter, moving in.

The professional’s fingers brushed the pistol’s grip.

I did not try to reach it. My hand closed on a length of broken roof beam, waterlogged and heavy as iron. As he rose, turning, I swung it in a short, brutal arc. It took him across the back of the knees.

He went down with a choked cry. I was on him, the beam across his throat. I leaned my weight into it. His eyes were wide, not with fear, but with a kind of furious disbelief. His hands clawed at the wood. His legs thrashed.

“The other one,” I hissed, my mouth close to his ear. “Call him off.”

He made a gurgling sound. His face was darkening.

“Call him!”

I eased the pressure, just a fraction.

“Alain!” he croaked. “Hold!”

Silence from outside. Then, “André?”

“Hold your fire!” André’s voice was a ragged scrape.

I kept the beam pressed. “How many?”

He stared up at me. The fury was gone, replaced by a cold, professional assessment. He was recalculating the odds, even here.

“Two,” he said. “Just us.”

“Rousseau’s instrument. A duet.”

“He pays for thoroughness.”

“He is going to be disappointed.”

I shifted my weight. The pain in my leg was a universe of fire. I could not hold this position for long. Alain was outside, a gun trained on the doorway. I had a hostage, but a hostage was only good if the other man cared. I did not know if Alain cared.

“You could kill me,” André said, his voice calmer now. “It would gain you a minute. Alain is sentimental. It would make him angry. He would be less careful. You might get a chance.”

“You are advising me on how to kill you?”

“I am explaining your options. They are poor.”

He was right. The calculus had shifted again. The needle was still in my hand, but the thread was tangled.

“I am not going to kill you,” I said.

“A mistake.”

“Probably.” I leaned closer. “Tell me about Rousseau. Not the official story. The real one. Why this? Why now?”

André’s eyes narrowed. “You do not know?”

“I know he is afraid. Fear makes men like him messy. Tell me what he fears.”

He was silent for a long moment. Then he gave a short, sharp laugh that turned into a cough against the beam. “The end. Not of the war. The end of the usefulness. The files, the lists… they are not just names. They are a ledger. Who was bought. Who was turned. Who was sacrificed. When the new men take the desks, they will open the ledger. They will need scapegoats. Rousseau does not intend to be an entry in his own book.”

So it was not just ambition. It was erasure. He was not trying to preserve the apparatus; he was trying to burn his own page in its history. The list was not a weapon for the future. It was a receipt for the past, and he needed to destroy it.

“And you?” I asked. “Where are you in his ledger?”

A shadow passed behind his eyes. “I am a tool. Tools are not entered. They are discarded.”

From outside, Alain’s voice, tense. “André! Talk to me or I come in!”

André’s gaze held mine. A understanding passed between us, bleak and absolute. We were both loose ends. His was just better paid.

“You have a car,” I said.

“Yes.”

“Where?”

“Half a kilometer west. On the forestry track.”

“Alain has the keys?”

“Yes.”

I made a decision. It was not a good one. It was the only one left on the board.

I lifted the beam from his throat. I rolled off him, onto my back, staring up at the patch of lightening sky through the shattered roof. I left myself utterly exposed.

André sat up, rubbing his neck. He looked at me, then at his pistol, lying in the filth. He did not reach for it.

“Alain!” he called, his voice regaining its command. “Stand down. We are coming out.”

He stood, slowly. He picked up his pistol, brushed it off, and slid it into his coat pocket. He did not offer me a hand. He simply looked down at me, a strange neutrality on his face.

“Get up,” he said.

It took me a full minute. I used the beam as a crutch, my leg screaming its protest, buckling twice before I found a precarious balance. I was finished. The run was over.

André led the way out of the hut. Alain was at the edge of the trees, a rifle held low. He was younger, with a nervous, sharp face. He looked from André to me, confused.

“What’s the play?” Alain asked.

“The play has changed,” André said. He walked toward him. “Give me the car keys.”

Alain hesitated, then fished a key from his pocket. André took it.

“Go back to the village. Call Rousseau. Tell him the subject is in custody. We are bringing him in.”

“I should come with you.”

“No. The car is small. He smells. I can handle him.” André’s tone brooked no argument. It was the voice of the senior partner closing a file.

Alain’s eyes flicked to me, full of a distrust that bordered on malice, but he nodded. He slung his rifle over his shoulder and began to walk south, back toward the river, without a backward glance.

André watched him until he was swallowed by the trees. Then he turned to me.

“The track is this way,” he said, and started walking west.

I followed, hobbling, the beam still in my hand. We did not speak. The woods grew lighter. Birds began their morning chatter, oblivious to the human wreckage moving through their domain.

After ten minutes of brutal progress, we broke out of the trees onto a rutted track. A black, unmarked Citroën Traction Avant was parked under an overhang of pine, nearly invisible. André unlocked the driver’s door.

“Get in,” he said.

I dropped the beam. I opened the passenger door and collapsed into the leather seat. The interior smelled of Gauloises and gun oil. André got in, started the engine. It purred to life, a smooth, powerful sound.

He did not drive back toward the river. He turned the car north, onto a narrower track that climbed away from the valley.

“This is not the way to Rousseau,” I said.

“No,” he said.

We drove in silence for a mile. The track joined a paved secondary road. He turned east, toward the rising sun.

“Where are we going?” I asked.

“Away,” he said. He lit a cigarette with one hand, the smoke filling the car. “You were correct. I am a tool. And Rousseau’s ledger has a page for tools that become complications. Alain’s call will buy us a few hours. Then Rousseau will know.”

“You are burning your own page.”

“I am choosing the fire,” he said. His profile was sharp against the morning light. “You have something Rousseau wants. I have something he fears. It is a start.”

“A start to what?”

“To staying off the page.” He glanced at me, the first real look of shared complicity. “The needle is in our hands now. Both of us. We will see if two loose ends can make a knot strong enough to hang a man like Rousseau.”

The car ate up the empty road. Behind us, the woods, the river, the hunter’s ground receded. Ahead was only the grey dawn and the unknown. The regime was dying, but its ghosts were multiplying. We were two of them now, riding into the light, not toward sanctuary, but toward a different kind of shadow. The hunt was over. The reckoning had just begun.



---

Chapter 22

CHAPTER 22

The road east was a grey scar through the pine forests of the Jura. The Traction Avant’s suspension was a miracle of French engineering, but every seam in the asphalt, every frost heave, sent a jolt up my spine and into the bad leg. The pain was a metronome, ticking off the kilometers. André drove with a quiet, surgical precision, his eyes rarely leaving the road, the Gauloise smoldering between his fingers. The smoke was a blue haze in the slanting morning light. We were a bubble of silence moving through a waking world.

I watched the forest blur past. The hunter’s ground was behind us, but the hunter’s calculus was not. Rousseau would have Alain’s report by now. He would know the trap had been sprung, and found empty. He would know his tool had broken in his hand. The ledger was open. Our names were being inked in.

“He will assume we run for a border,” I said. My voice was rough, unused. “Switzerland. Or Germany.”

André took a final drag and flicked the butt out his window. “He will assume correctly. For the first hour. Then he will widen the net. Airports. Train stations. Safe houses he believes I do not know about.” He glanced at the fuel gauge. “We have the advantage of the first move. And of being insignificant.”

“Insignificant?”

“You are a damaged foreigner with a grudge. I am a disgraced functionary. In the grand scheme of the Fifth Republic’s birth pangs, we are a footnote. Rousseau’s personal footnote, but a footnote nonetheless. He will use his own resources first. The official channels come later, when pride is exhausted. That gives us perhaps a day.”

“To do what?”

“To become significant,” he said.

He reached behind his seat, his hand groping blindly, and pulled a folded, grease-stained map onto my lap. It was a Michelin road map of eastern France. “Find us a saint,” he said.

I unfolded it. The paper crackled. “A what?”

“A town. A village. Named for a saint. Isolated. Not on a main road.”

I traced the web of red and yellow lines. The names blurred: Besançon, Pontarlier, Morteau. The smaller ones, in tiny script, were hymns to forgotten martyrs. Saint-Laurent-en-Grandvaux. Saint-Claude. Saint-Lupicin. I put my finger on one, nestled in a knot of secondary roads south of Lons-le-Saunier. “Saint-Amour.”

André almost smiled. “Too poetic. And too obvious. Keep looking.”

My eyes travelled south, toward the Alps. A cluster of blue lines indicated lakes. There, at the tip of one: Saint-Point. “Here. Lac de Saint-Point. It’s a dead end. One road in, along the western shore. Villages scattered. Remote.”

He considered for a moment. “Good. A dead end is a place people stop looking past. They assume you have moved through. We will stop.”

“What’s at Saint-Point?”

“A man who is not a friend,” André said. “An acquaintance of mutual inconvenience. He owes me a debt that is not monetary. He will give us one night. No more.”

The specificity was its own form of trust. He was not just driving; he was navigating a landscape of old favors and older betrayals. I refolded the map. “And after the night?”

“We use the needle,” he said. The word hung in the smoky air. “Rousseau wants it because it is a key. To what, you have not said.”

I looked out at the passing pines. The truth was a cold stone in my gut. I had carried it from Casablanca, through the rain-slick streets and the quiet terror of the clinic, across the Mediterranean into this decaying heart of empire. I had not said it because to say it was to make it real, to give it a shape that could be hunted.
“It’s not a list of agents,” I said. “Not in the conventional sense.”

André was silent, waiting.

“It’s a ledger of souls,” I said. The words came out flat, toneless. “A census. Taken by a man who believed he was building a new world, and needed to know who was fit to inherit it.”

“A census,” André repeated, the word neutral.

“Of children,” I said. “Moroccan children. Orphans, mostly. Boys from the bled, from the bidonvilles. Taken in by a certain charitable foundation run by a certain colonel with anthropological pretensions. He measured their skulls. Tested their reflexes. Assessed their ‘aptitude for loyalty.’ He filed them away. Rousseau’s private reserve. His next generation of quiet, grateful men. The needle contains the master register. Names. Locations. Photographs. The original is ash. This is the only copy.”

The only sound was the hum of the tires. André’s knuckles were white on the wheel.

“He is not just cleaning house,” he said finally. “He is safeguarding his legacy. His future. These boys are now men. Scattered, perhaps. But some will be in places of use. A clerk in a ministry. A policeman in a growing city. A soldier. A lifetime of implied debt, waiting to be called.” He let out a slow breath. “This is not an embarrassment. This is a root system. To expose it is not to prune a branch. It is to salt the earth.”

“Yes.”

“And you wish to expose it.”

“I wish to destroy it,” I said. “To put it in the hands of someone who will see that every name is lost, every file erased. That the debt is cancelled.”

“A noble sentiment,” André said, and his tone was not mocking, merely weary. “But sentiment does not erase ledgers. Power does. You need a counterweight. A power that fears Rousseau’s future more than it values his present.”

“Who?”

“That,” he said, “is the question we must answer before our night is through.”

We left the Jura behind, descending into the richer, rolling farmland of the Ain. The Traction Avant was conspicuous in its anonymity—the perfect unmarked car for officials who did not wish to be seen. We avoided towns, sticking to farm roads that wound between fields of dormant vines and muddy pastures. Just past noon, André pulled into the yard of a silent, shuttered agricultural co-operative. He emerged from the car with a set of lock picks, and within five minutes was back with two heavy jerry cans of petrol. He siphoned the dregs from the Traction’s tank into a ditch and refilled it from the cans. He did not speak during the operation. It was routine. The routine of a man who had made a career of slipping through cracks.

Back on the road, he produced a bag of apples and a hunk of hard cheese from the glove compartment. We ate as we drove. The food was tasteless, fuel.

It was late afternoon when we saw the first sign for Lac de Saint-Point. The sky had lowered, a lid of uniform grey. The air grew colder, damper. The road began to climb again, into foothills where the pines returned, thicker and darker. Then, between the trunks, a flash of steel-grey water, vast and still.

The village of Saint-Point was a straggle of stone houses and a single, sharp-spired church clinging to the steep western shore. It was the colour of the sky and the lake, a study in grey. Desolate, beautiful. André drove past it, following the narrow lakeshore road south. We passed a few other hamlets, names on signs: Malbuisson, Grange-de-Mont. Tourist places in season, now hibernating.

A few kilometers further, a dirt track branched off the road, descending through a copse of bare birch trees toward a boathouse. André turned onto it. The Citroën bounced and slithered in the ruts. The boathouse was a low, sagging structure of weathered planks, its doors padlocked. Beyond it, on a slight promontory, stood a small, square stone house with a slate roof. Smoke trickled from its chimney.

André killed the engine. The silence was immediate and profound, broken only by the lap of water against stone and the distant cry of a gull. “Wait here,” he said.

He got out and walked toward the house, his footsteps crunching on the gravel path. He did not go to the front door. He went around the side and knocked on a window. A minute passed. Then a curtain twitched. Another minute, and the front door opened.

A man stood there, silhouetted against the firelight within. He was tall, stooped, wearing a thick fisherman’s sweater. He and André did not shake hands. They spoke for a moment, their voices too low to carry. The man looked past André, at the car. His gaze felt like a physical pressure. Then he gave a single, curt nod and stepped back inside. André returned to the car.

“One night,” he said, opening his door. “He has a shed for the car. We push it.”

Between my leg and his city-strength, it was a clumsy, painful operation, but we managed to roll the Traction Avant into a dilapidated wood shed behind the house, covering it with a mildewed canvas tarp. Our host appeared at the shed door as we finished. Up close, he was older than I’d thought, his face a roadmap of seams and weathered grooves, his eyes the pale, washed-out blue of the lake in winter. He looked at me, then at my leg.

“You bring trouble to my quiet,” he said. His voice was gravel, his accent from the north, maybe Lille.

“I return a favor, Pascal,” André said, brushing wood dust from his trousers. “The trouble is mine. It will not stay the night.”

Pascal’s eyes didn’t leave me. “The foreigner has the look of a man who carries trouble in his pocket.”

“He carries a headache for people who deserve one,” André said. “We need food, a fire, and no questions.”

Pascal snorted. “The food you will get. The fire is lit. The questions I will keep to myself. They are less trouble.” He turned and hobbled back toward the house. “Come in before you freeze. The wind comes off the lake like a knife.”

The interior of the house was one large room, a combined kitchen, living, and sleeping space dominated by a massive stone fireplace. A pot hung over the flames, emitting a smell of stewed game and herbs. A bed was tucked into an alcove, and a table with two chairs stood under a window looking onto the grey water. Books were stacked everywhere, their spines cracked. It was the retreat of a man who had chosen absence.

Pascal served us bowls of thick venison stew and mugs of bitter black coffee. He did not eat with us. He took his bowl to a stool by the fire and ate in silence, staring into the flames. The meal was hot, rich, and for a few minutes, the world shrank to the warmth of the fire and the simple act of sustenance.

When we were done, André took our bowls to the sink. Pascal spoke without turning from the fire.

“The radio says there was an incident near the Swiss border. A police action. Hunters reported gunfire. They are looking for two men. The descriptions are vague. But they mention a limp.”

André did not react. He dried the bowls meticulously. “What else?”

“That is all. It is a small item. Buried. The sort of thing that grows.”

“Thank you, Pascal.”

The old man finally turned. His pale eyes were sharp. “The debt is cleared, André. After tonight, we are square. You do not know this place. I have not seen you.”

“Understood.”

Pascal nodded, seemingly satisfied. He banked the fire, took a heavy coat from a hook, and picked up a lantern. “I will be in the boathouse. There is a cot. The house is yours until first light. Then you are gone.” He let himself out into the gathering dusk. The door closed with a solid, final sound.

André waited until the glow of the lantern had disappeared down the path. Then he went to the bed alcove, knelt, and pried up a floorboard. From the space beneath, he withdrew a bulky, military-style field radio, its casing scratched and dented. He carried it to the table and set it up, plugging it into a socket near the fireplace. The dials glowed a faint orange.

“Pascal’s one vice,” he said, seeing my look. “He likes to listen to the world he left. It helps him appreciate the silence.”

He put on a headset and began to turn the dial, a faint crackle of static filling the pauses between the hiss of the fire. I watched the lake through the window. The last of the light was leaching from the sky, turning the water to ink. A single light appeared on the far shore, a pinprick of yellow in the vast grey.

After twenty minutes, André took off the headset. His face was grim. “It is as Pascal said. The report is small, but it is on the wire. A ‘security operation’ in the Jura. No details. But Rousseau has planted the seed. By morning, every gendarme in a hundred kilometers will have the sketch. Our car is known.”

“Can we switch it?”

“Not here. Not without time and tools we do not have.” He lit a cigarette, the flame of his match a sudden, violent bloom in the dim room. “We must change the calculus. Move from being hunted to being hunters. To do that, we need to wield the needle. Not just possess it.”

“You said we need a counterweight. A power that fears his future.”

“Yes. The question is which one. The SDECE is a nest of vipers, but Rousseau is a prized viper. The DST? Possibly. They are the internal security service, jealous of their territory. They would love a scandal that humiliates the external service. But they are also notoriously leaky.” He blew smoke at the ceiling. “The political sphere? A minister? Too volatile. They would trade the information for a favor, and we would end up in the Seine.”

“What about the press?” I asked.

André gave a short, mirthless laugh. “In France? The newspapers that matter are in the pockets of the men Rousseau serves. They would spike the story and send the proofs to his desk with a bow. No. We need an institution with its own power, its own longevity, and a profound distaste for Rousseau’s kind of social engineering.”

He fell silent, thinking. I watched the smoke from his cigarette curl and dissolve in the firelight. The answer, when it came, was not from him. It was from the radio.

A new voice had crackled to life on the speaker André had left on low volume. A news bulletin from the state broadcaster. The lead item was about Algeria. Talks, posturing, the usual grim stalemate. Then the announcer’s tone shifted, to one of sober respect.

“...and from Rome, the Vatican announced today that His Holiness has appointed a new special envoy to liaise with the French government on matters of charitable works and cultural preservation in North Africa. The envoy, Monsignor Valère, is expected to arrive in Paris next week...”

André’s head snapped up. He stared at the radio as if it had bitten him. He slowly reached over and turned the volume down until the voice was a whisper, then nothing.

“Of course,” he said softly. “The one ledger older than all others.”

“The Church?”

“Not the Church,” he said. “A specific man. Monsignor Valère.” He stubbed out his cigarette with precise force. “He is a diplomat, yes. But before that, he was a priest in Oran. During the war. He ran an orphanage. He saw what the colons did. What the FLN did. What the French army did. He has a particular, and personal, hatred for anyone who uses children as pawns. He has written about it. Theological critiques of ‘the engineering of souls.’ Rousseau would have read them. And despised them.”

“You think he would use the information?”

“I think he is the only man in Europe with the moral authority, the international reach, and the sheer, bureaucratic stubbornness to see it through. The Vatican does not forget. It moves slowly, but it grinds fine. It is a counterweight Rousseau cannot buy, cannot intimidate, and cannot ignore.”

“How do we get it to him?”

“He is in Rome. We are here. Rousseau will have every route to Italy watched.” André stood and began to pace the small room, his energy suddenly electric. “We do not go to him. We make him come to us. To something he cannot refuse.”

“How?”

André stopped at the window, looking out at the black lake. “We give him a preview. A single page from the ledger. We transmit it. Not the whole thing—a fragment. Enough to prove its nature, its horror. We send it to him through a channel he trusts, but Rousseau cannot easily intercept.”

“What channel?”

He turned from the window. “The Mission de France. A Catholic publishing house in Lyon. Conservative, scholarly. Valère contributes to their journals. They are a backchannel for sensitive Vatican matters. Their security is ecclesiastical, not state. Rousseau’s men would not think to monitor them closely, not at first.”

It was a plan. Fragile, riddled with ifs, but it was a vector. A direction. “We need the needle,” I said. “To extract a page.”

André went to his coat, which was draped over a chair. From an inner pocket, he withdrew the steel tube. He placed it on the rough wood of the table. It looked innocuous. A piece of technical debris. He then took a multi-tool from his pocket and unfolded a pair of needle-nose pliers.

“The mechanism is simple,” he said. “A spring-loaded spindle. The film is wound inside. To extract a single frame without exposing the whole roll requires light, and precision.” He looked around the room. His eyes settled on the fireplace. “We need a darkroom. A makeshift one.”

He began to give orders, his voice low and urgent. I followed them, my leg screaming in protest. We hung blankets over the windows, sealing out the last hints of dusk. We moved the table to the center of the room. André took a clean white dish from the cupboard. From his own bag, he produced a small, flat case. Inside were a photographer’s loupe, a pair of white cotton gloves, and several empty 35mm film canisters.

“Pascal develops his own pictures of birds,” André said by way of explanation, pulling on the gloves. “He will not miss these.”

He used the pliers to grip the end cap of the needle. With a firm, careful twist, it unscrewed. He tipped the tube gently. A small, tightly wound spool of microfilm slid into his gloved palm. It was smaller than a thumbnail, a dense, shiny coil. He placed it in the dish.

“The fire,” he said. “We need a safe light. Only the embers.”

I used a poker to push the burning logs aside, exposing the bed of deep-red coals. Their glow was a somber, pulsing light. It was enough to see by, but André explained that microfilm was orthochromatic—less sensitive to red light. It was a risk, but the only one we had.

In the crimson gloom, he became a silhouette of intense concentration. Using the tip of a knife blade, he teased the end of the microfilm strip free from the spool. He scanned it with the loupe, his face inches from the dish.

“The ledger is chronological,” he whispered. “Each frame is a dossier. Name. Photograph. Physical data. Notes on ‘disposition’ and ‘placement.’” His voice was tight. “Here. This one. A boy from Marrakesh. Orphaned in the ’53 riots. ‘Subject 17.’ Assigned to a school in Meknes. ‘Shows high compliance. Suitable for administrative conditioning.’” He looked up, his eyes black pools in the red light. “This is the one. It has everything. The coldness of it. The photograph is clear.”

“Can you cut it?”

“We do not cut. We copy.” He took a fresh, empty spool from his case. With painstaking slowness, using the knife and pliers, he wound the microfilm forward from the master spool to the new one, counting the sprocket holes under his breath. He stopped at the frame after the one he wanted. “Now. This is the frame. I will re-roll the master, but this single frame will be on the copy spool. We will sacrifice one image to save the rest.”

His hands were steady. He completed the operation, separating the single frame onto the smaller spool, then carefully rewound the master ledger back into the steel needle. He screwed the cap back on and handed it to me. It felt warmer than before.

“Now we make it readable,” he said. He went to Pascal’s shelves, found a heavy magnifying glass. He laid the single frame of microfilm on the table. Using the magnifying glass to focus the dim red light from the fireplace onto the film, he projected a blurred, inverted image onto a sheet of white paper I held steady. It was a ghostly, haunting negative: the wide, solemn eyes of a young boy, next to a typed form filled with neat, clinical script.

“We need a positive. A photograph to send.” He looked at the clock on the mantle. It was past eight. “There is a pharmacy in Malbuisson. It might have a Polaroid camera for tourists. Or at least photographic paper and basic chemicals. Pascal can get it.”

“He said no more questions. No more involvement.”

“He will do this,” André said. He went to the door, opened it, and whistled sharply into the night. A few minutes later, the bobbing lantern returned up the path.

Pascal listened to André’s request in the doorway, the cold lake wind swirling into the room. His expression did not change. “A Polaroid. Chemicals. This is not part of the debt.”

“It is a new debt,” André said. “One I will repay. Name it.”

Pascal’s pale eyes flicked to me, to the magnifying glass on the table, to the tiny sliver of film. “The truth of what you are doing. One sentence.”

André did not hesitate. “We are sending a ghost to haunt a man who thought he could own the future.”

Pascal considered this. He gave a slow, almost imperceptible nod. “The pharmacy closed at seven. Old man Girod lives above it. He is a friend. He will open for me. For a price.” He held out his hand.

André took out his wallet. It was thick with francs. He gave Pascal all of it. Pascal counted it, tucked it away. “One hour,” he said, and disappeared back into the night.

The wait was an agony of stillness. André cleaned and repacked the radio. I sat by the fire, the needle a hard cylinder in my hand. The single frame of microfilm, on its tiny spool, lay on the table like a condemned man’s last meal.

Pascal returned in fifty-three minutes. He carried a brown paper bag. From it, he produced a Polaroid Land Camera, bulky and new, a pack of film, a bottle of developer, a bottle of fixer, three small trays, and a packet of glossy photographic paper.

“Girod asked no questions,” Pascal said. “The price was high. The debt is now to him. You owe me nothing. I owe him. This displeases me.” He set the items on the table. “The darkroom you have made will suffice. The chemicals are simple. Follow the instructions on the bottles. I will be in the boathouse. At first light, you and your ghost are gone.”

He left again, taking his lantern, plunging the room back into the hellish red glow.

André worked quickly, mixing the chemicals in the trays. He cut a single sheet of photographic paper. In the dark, using the magnifying glass and the coal-light as an enlarger, he exposed the microfilm image onto the paper. He slid it into the developer tray. We watched, our heads close together, as the image swam out of the white nothingness. The boy’s face emerged first, then the cruel, neat typing. It was a stark, black-and-white crime scene photo of a stolen life.

He fixed it, rinsed it in water from the pump at the sink, and blotted it dry with a clean cloth. He held it up to the firelight. It was perfect. A damning fragment.

The Polaroid was for the transmission. André loaded the camera, pointed it at the finished photograph where it lay on the table, and fired the flash. The camera whirred and ejected a square, blank sheet. We waited the agonizing sixty seconds, and then, like magic, the image began to coalesce: a photograph of a photograph, slightly blurred at the edges, but the content unmistakable.

It was time.

André set up the radio again. He tuned it to a specific frequency, one he knew from memory. He attached a device to the side of the set—a makeshift scanner he assembled from parts in his bag. He placed the Polaroid on the flatbed.

“This will take the image and convert it to a slow-scan television signal. It will be a series of tones. The Mission de France has the equipment to decode it. They will think it is a test, or a misdirect, until they see the content. They will know what to do. They will get it to Valère.”

He began to transmit. The radio emitted a series of strange, warbling chirps and drones. It sounded like a mechanical bird dying. It went on for ten minutes. Then he stopped, changed frequency, and repeated the sequence. He did this three times, on three different bands, to ensure it was heard.

When he was finished, he dismantled the scanner and put the radio away. The original photograph, the microfilm frame, and the Polaroid he placed in an envelope he found in Pascal’s drawer. He addressed it in a neat, anonymous hand to the Mission de France, Lyon. He stamped it with a stamp from his own wallet.

“We will post this in the morning. From a different town. A redundancy.”

He looked exhausted. The red light hollowed his cheeks. We had done it. We had fired the first shot. Not with a bullet, but with a boy’s face.

“Now we wait,” I said.

“Now we move,” he corrected. “The transmission will be intercepted by others, even if they cannot decode it. Rousseau’s men monitor all unusual signals. They will get a directional fix. It will point them here, to this side of the lake. We have until dawn, perhaps less.”

He was right. The act of reaching out had made us more visible, not less. We were a flicker on a screen now.

We spent the next hours in a state of tense readiness. We did not sleep. We packed our few things. André cleaned every surface we had touched. At four in the morning, with the night at its deepest and coldest, we heard it.

Not from the road. From the lake.

A low, rhythmic thrum, growing steadily louder. The sound of an inboard motor, throttled back to a murmur.

André was at the window in an instant, peering through a gap in the blanket. I joined him. Out on the ink-black water, a shape was materializing. A dark, low launch, without running lights. It was heading directly for Pascal’s boathouse.

“They are efficient,” André whispered. “They triangulated the signal and came by water. Quiet. Unseen.”

We watched as the launch ghosted up to the boathouse dock. Three figures disembarked, silhouettes against the faint sheen of the water. They moved with purpose toward the boathouse. We heard a door being forced, a short, sharp cry that was cut off. Pascal.

André was already moving. “The car. Now.”

We grabbed our things. He took the envelope. I had the needle. We slipped out the back door into the biting cold. The shed seemed a mile away. Every crunch of gravel underfoot sounded like an explosion. We reached it, threw off the tarp. I got in the passenger side, my heart hammering against my ribs. André put his shoulder to the rear of the car and pushed, rocking it silently out of the shed onto the dirt path.

From the direction of the boathouse, a flashlight beam stabbed the darkness, sweeping across the house.

André got in, released the handbrake. The car began to roll silently down the slight incline, away from the house, toward the main lakeshore road. He let it coast for fifty meters before he turned the ignition. The engine coughed, then roared to life.

Immediately, shouts echoed from behind us. A flashlight beam pinned us for a second. Then the first shot rang out, a flat crack that shattered the lake’s silence. The rear window webbed into a spider’s nest of cracks.

André slammed the gears and stamped on the accelerator. The Traction Avant lurched forward, its headlights slicing the dark as we hit the paved road. He turned north, away from Saint-Point village, back the way we had come.

In the mirror, I saw the launch’s lights flare to life. It was turning, churning a white wake in the black water, giving chase. But we were on the winding road, and they were on the straight lake. They would have to find a place to land, to intercept.

“They will call ahead,” André said, his voice calm, his hands fierce on the wheel. “Roadblocks. On every route out of the hills.”

The car screamed through the night, headlights painting the pine trunks in frantic, sweeping strokes. The needle was in my hand. The boy’s face was in an envelope on the seat between us. We had sent our ghost. Now we had to outrun its keepers.

The reckoning had begun. And we were no longer just running from it. We were dragging it, howling, into the light. The road ahead was a black tunnel. At the end of it, a man in Rome would see a photograph. And a man in Paris would feel the first, cold draft of a door he had tried forever to lock, swinging open on its hinges.



---

Chapter 23

CHAPTER 23

The road was a black artery, and we were the fever moving through it. The Traction Avant’s engine was a sustained, metallic scream, a sound that seemed to tear at the fabric of the night itself. André drove with a kind of brutal grace, his body a tense extension of the machine, feeling for the limits of adhesion on the damp tarmac. The cracked rear window was a mosaic of our escape, each fracture holding a sliver of reflected headlight or the distant, stubborn gleam of the lake.

My hands were busy. The adrenaline that had flooded me at the boathouse was receding, leaving behind the cold, sharp clarity of a surgical theater. I reloaded the Walther from the spare magazine in my pocket, the click of the slide a small, definitive punctuation in the roaring cabin. I placed it on the seat beside the envelope. The boy’s face watched from between the folds, a silent passenger.

“They will not use the gendarmes,” André said, not taking his eyes from the serpentine road. “Not for this. They will use their own men. Corsicans. Ex-paras. They will have cars. They will know these roads.”

“How many ways out of the hills?”

“Three. This one, north to Morez. The one east to Saint-Claude. The one south, back toward Lons-le-Saunier. They will block all three. A net. They are not policemen. They will shoot first and invent the reason after.”

The plan, such as it was, had been to reach Geneva. A neutral city, a nest of banks and shadows where the photograph could be transmitted, where the leverage could be applied from a distance. It was a plan made in the quiet of the Parisian apartment, over maps and coffee. It was a plan that assumed the opposition would move with bureaucratic slowness, not with the violent speed of men whose entire world was threatened.

The colonial aftermath was not just a setting; it was a methodology. It was the habit of control, the reflex of suppression. The man in Paris, Vauban, had learned his trade putting down riots in Algiers, pacifying villages in the Rif. He would apply the same principles now: isolate, encircle, eliminate. We were no longer investigators. We were insurgents in his occupied territory.

The first roadblock appeared around a blind bend, a tableau staged with efficient menace. Two black Citroën DS cars were angled across the road, their noses almost touching. Men stood in the glare of their headlights, silhouettes with the bulky outlines of submachine guns slung across their chests. One held a red lantern, swinging it slowly in a stop motion. It was a quarter-mile ahead.

André did not slow. He downshifted, the engine’s scream pitching higher.

“Hold on,” he said, and his voice was flat, devoid of drama.

He flicked off our headlights.

The world plunged into a rushing, ink-black void. The only light came from the roadblock’s own beams, painting the pine trunks in a static, theatrical wash. The men’s faces were pale ovals, turning toward the sound of our approach. For a second, they were confused, searching the darkness.

André aimed for the gap between the rear of one DS and the stone embankment on the left side of the road. It was a space not meant for a car. He was calculating the physics of desperation.

A shout. The lantern was thrown aside. The silhouettes brought their weapons up.

The first burst of automatic fire was a string of bright, muzzle-flash pearls in the dark. It went high, chewing into the branches above us. The second was lower. I heard the thwack-thwack-thwack of rounds punching into metal, the shriek of a ricochet. The passenger window beside me dissolved into a cascade of glass pellets.

Then we hit.

The impact was a sickening, grinding shove. The Traction Avant’s rear quarter panel sheared along the Citroën’s bumper with a scream of tormented steel. Our back end swung wide, the rear tires scrabbling for purchase on the gravel shoulder. For a heart-stopping moment, I saw the steep drop into the ravine beyond the low stone wall, a maw of darkness. André fought the wheel, muscles corded in his neck, feeding power to the front wheels, dragging us back onto the tarmac. We clipped the wall. A shower of stone sparks flew past my window.

And then we were through, accelerating again into the darkness. He flipped the headlights back on. The beams revealed a road empty save for our own wreckage.

In the mirror, the roadblock was a receding constellation of light and movement. One man was on a radio. They would not follow immediately. One of their cars was crippled. But the net had been touched. The whole system would now contract.

“Damage?” André asked. His breathing was slightly elevated. That was all.

I craned my neck. “Left rear tire is losing air. Sounds like a fender rubbing. Nothing vital hit.”

He nodded. “They will have called ahead. The next one will be heavier. Or they will simply mine the road.”

The needle of the speedometer hovered at a dangerous number. The wind howled through the missing window, cold and smelling of pine resin and burnt gunpowder. My leg was a solid bar of pain, the old injury singing in harmony with the fresh terror. I welcomed it. It was a focus, a tether to the physical reality of the moment. This was not Fez. This was not a passive accident. This was a choice, met head-on.

“We cannot reach Geneva,” I said.

“No.”

“The net is for the roads. Not for the land.”

He glanced at me, his face a mask of shadows and sharp angles carved by the dashboard lights. “The land is forest and rock. It goes straight up.”

“There are paths. Hunters’ paths. Smugglers’ paths.” I was thinking of the maps we had studied, the contour lines like fingerprints of the earth. I was thinking of the Rif, of the way a man could disappear into a landscape that refused to be mapped by colonizers. “They expect a car. They will not expect two men on foot, moving cross-country.”

“Your leg.”

“Will carry me. Or it won’t. But the car will not carry us through another blockade.”

He was silent for a half-mile, the calculation happening behind his eyes. He was a creature of the city, of the safe house and the parked car and the dead drop. The wilderness was an alien, hostile geography. But he was also a pragmatist.

“Where?”

“The Col de la Faucille. It is the highest pass before the border. Above the treeline. There is a cable station, for summer tourists. It will be closed, deserted. From there, we can see the whole valley. We can find a way down the Swiss side.”

“It is fifteen kilometers. Straight up.”

“We have until dawn,” I said. “And we have the one thing they do not.”

“What is that?”

“We know what we are running from. They are only guessing.”

He saw the sense in it. The grim, hopeless sense. He took the next turning, a smaller road that snaked away from the main route, indicated by a weathered sign for a ferme-auberge. The pavement soon gave way to gravel, then to a rutted track used for logging. The damaged tire was fully flat now, the rim grinding a discordant rhythm on the stones. We drove until the track became impassable, the car lurching into a ditch where a stream had washed out the path.

We got out into the profound silence of the deep woods. The engine’s echo died, replaced by the drip of water from the branches and the distant, cold whisper of the wind higher up. I took the envelope, tucked it inside my jacket. I checked the Walther. André retrieved a rucksack from the trunk—his emergency kit: water, a first-aid tin, wire-cutters, a torch.

We left the Traction Avant like a dead animal, its steel skin still ticking as it cooled. We entered the trees.

The first hour was a battle of will against physiology. The path was a faint indentation, slick with fallen needles and moss. Every upward step sent a jolt of fire from my knee to my spine. I used a broken branch as a staff, a third leg. My breathing was ragged, a loud, shameful sound in the holy quiet of the forest. André moved like a ghost ahead of me, pausing occasionally to listen, his head cocked. We heard nothing but the woods. No engines. No shouts. The net was on the roads, far below us now.

We were in the interstitial space, the porous border between the controlled territory and the wild. This was the true colonial aftermath: not the grand boulevards of Casablanca or the ministries of Paris, but these forgotten places where the old rules of power frayed and snapped. Here, a man with a bad leg and a photograph could still move, could still exist.

The trees began to thin. The air grew sharper, thinner. The pain in my leg had settled into a constant, grinding ache, a background noise I could almost ignore. We emerged above the treeline into a world of moonlit rock and scree. The sky was vast, littered with hard, cold stars. Below us, the valley was a bowl of darkness, pricked here and there by the lonely lights of farms. Far to the north, a constellation of brighter lights marked the town of Gex, and beyond that, the invisible line of the Swiss border.

The Col de la Faucille was a bald, wind-scoured saddle between two granite shoulders. The cable station was a hulking, modernistic shape of concrete and glass, a spaceship stranded in the primordial landscape. It was dark, locked up for the winter. We approached from the rear, using the cover of a service shed.

André used the wire-cutters on a padlock. The door to the maintenance bay swung open with a groan. Inside, it was pitch black and smelled of grease and cold concrete. He risked the torch, its beam cutting a narrow swath through the darkness. The place was a cavern of silent machinery—the great drums for the cables, tool benches, a parked snowcat on treads.

We climbed a metal staircase to the observation level, a glass-walled room that offered a panoramic view of the Alps. In the moonlight, the peaks were like the teeth of a fossilized jawbone, stretching into infinity. It was a view meant to inspire awe. To us, it was a tactical map.

André swept the beam across the Swiss side. The slopes were less severe, dotted with the dark shapes of ski chalets. A single access road switchbacked up from the valley floor, ending at a closed gate.

“There,” he said. “We follow the ski runs down. We can be at that road in two hours. From there, we can steal a car. Be in Geneva by mid-morning.”

It was a clean line. A possible line. We had slipped the net. We had a path to the endgame.

That was when the lights came on.

Not the lights in the station. The lights outside.

Powerful, halogen-bright beams mounted on the corners of the building blazed to life, flooding the rocky plateau around the station with a harsh, blue-white glare. We were trapped in a glass box, fully illuminated.

From the shadows behind the service shed, figures emerged. Not the silhouettes of the roadblock. These men moved with a disciplined, military precision. They wore dark, civilian clothing, but they carried MAT-49 submachine guns with the casual familiarity of soldiers. There were six of them. They fanned out, covering the exits.

A seventh man walked out into the center of the lit circle. He was tall, wearing a long, dark wool coat. His hands were in his pockets. He looked up at the glass wall, directly at where we stood.

It was Vauban.

He had not stayed in Paris. He had come to the borderlands himself. The hunter, when the prey is important enough, leaves his desk.

André killed the torch. We were back in relative darkness, but it was a futile gesture. He had seen us. We were specimens in a lit diorama.

“The back stairs,” André whispered, his voice taut. “The maintenance tunnel for the cables. It leads down the pylon on the Swiss side.”

It was a vertical shaft. A climb for engineers. For a man with a ruined knee, it was a pipe dream.

“You go,” I said.

He looked at me.

“Take this.” I handed him the envelope. “Get it to your man in Rome. That is the only thing that matters now.”

“I am not leaving you here.”

“You are not leaving me. I am staying. He wants the photograph. He wants the witness. I am both. I will give him a conversation. You will give him a catastrophe.”

Below, Vauban raised a hand. One of his men lifted a bullhorn. The amplified voice, distorted and metallic, echoed across the stone basin.

“Come out. There is no path down that is not covered. Come out, and we will talk.”

The voice was calm, reasonable. It was the voice of the administration, offering terms. It was the most terrifying sound I had ever heard.

André’s face was a struggle. His duty was to the mission. His instinct, I saw with a sudden, clarifying shock, was to the man beside him. We had passed from allies into something else, something forged in the dark shed and the screaming car and this silent climb.

“The tunnel,” I insisted. “Now. While he is talking. It is the only move left.”

He took the envelope. He did it with a slow, deliberate motion, as if accepting a sacred charge. Then he did something else. He reached into his rucksack and pressed the cold, hard shape of the wire-cutters into my hand. Not a weapon. A tool.

“Do not make it easy for him,” he said.

Then he was gone, melting back into the darkness of the machinery room, toward the hatch that led to the service tunnel.

I was alone.

I looked at the wire-cutters in my hand. I looked at the Walther in my pocket. Six men with automatic weapons. A seventh who was the architect of all this.

I walked to the glass door that led onto the observation deck. I unlocked it. The wind hit me like a physical blow, sharp and icy, carrying the scent of snow and stone. I stepped out into the blaze of the lights.

I raised my empty hands.

Vauban watched me. He made a small, almost imperceptible gesture. His men did not lower their weapons, but their fingers came off the triggers. He took his hands from his pockets and started walking toward the base of the station.

I began the long, slow descent down the external metal staircase. Each step was a negotiation with pain, a public display of vulnerability. I let it show. I let him see the limp, the careful, painful grip on the railing. I was the wounded animal, leaving the cover of the trees. He was the calm, patient hunter.

I reached the bottom. The wind flapped my jacket. The lights were blinding. I could see nothing beyond their circumference.

Vauban stopped ten feet from me. He was older than I had imagined from his file. In his late fifties, with a lean, patrician face that had been handsome before it was carved into austerity by years of command and compromise. His eyes were the color of flint. He assessed me, not as a threat, but as a problem that had finally been contained.

“Mr. Carrefour,” he said. His voice was not the bullhorn’s distortion. It was cultured, quiet, carrying perfectly in the thin air. “Or do you prefer the other name? The one from before Fez?”

He knew. Of course he knew. He had pulled my thread the moment I had tugged on his.

“Carrefour is fine,” I said. “It has fewer bad memories.”

A ghost of a smile touched his lips. “We are all collections of bad memories by a certain age. It is the price of paying attention.” He glanced up at the observation deck. “Your companion. The one who drives with such… conviction. He is not joining us?”

“He prefers the view from above.”

“He will find it limited shortly.” Vauban took a step closer. He did not smell of fear or exertion. He smelled of good wool and a faint, spicy cologne. “You have something that belongs to me. A piece of paper. A fiction.”

“It didn’t feel like a fiction to the man who gave his life for it. Or to the boy in the picture.”

The flint in his eyes hardened. “Sentiment. The great weakness. That boy is a ghost. A story told by bitter men who lost their world. My world. The world of order. Of France.”

“Your world was built on a lie. On a child’s body in a well.”

He shrugged, a small, elegant movement. “Empires are not built on truths. They are built on necessary stories. That story became… inconvenient. A loose thread. I clipped it. Now you have pulled it back out. Why? You are not a crusader. You are a man with a bad leg who looks for other people’s secrets because he has none of his own worth keeping.”

He was good. He was aiming for the nerve, the private shame. The colonial mindset: first, understand the subject’s weakness. Then exploit it.

“I was hired to find a truth,” I said. “I found it. That’s the job.”

“The job is over. You are now in the realm of consequences.” He looked past me, at his men. “We will go inside. Out of this wind. You will give me the photograph. You will tell me the names of everyone you have spoken to. And then we will discuss what happens next.”

“What happens next is the photograph reaches Rome. And a newspaper in Paris gets a copy. The door swings open, Vauban. You can feel the draft.”

For the first time, a flicker of irritation crossed his face. The calm administrator facade cracked, revealing the iron will beneath. “Nothing is reaching Rome. My men are on every road, at every border post. Your friend, if he even made it off this mountain, will be in a ditch before noon. The door you speak of is sealed. Permanently.”

He believed it. That was his weakness. The absolute conviction that his control was total, that the system he served and had shaped would function perfectly. He could not conceive of a leak he could not plug, a thread he could not cut.

He gestured toward the station entrance. “Inside.”

I didn’t move. I held up the wire-cutters. “You’ll want these.”

He frowned. “What?”

“For the fence. The one around the transmitter array on the roof.” I pointed vaguely upward with the cutters. “Your men will have to cut through it to disable the equipment.”

His gaze followed the gesture, then snapped back to me. His eyes narrowed, calculating. “What transmitter?”
